Swallowing a Grain from Outside the Mouth: A Case of Eating During Fasting
Ruling statement on swallowing a grain from outside the mouth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing a grain from outside the mouth during fasting and explain why it requires both qada' and kaffarah.

Core Idea
Even a single grain from outside the mouth breaks the fast and requires kaffarah because it constitutes eating.

In the chapter on what does not break the fast, the author immediately presents an exception: swallowing a grain of wheat or sesame from outside the mouth. This ruling is given as the chosen opinion (al-mukhtar) of the later Hanafi authorities. The key principle is that any food substance entering the throat from outside the body is considered eating, which invalidates the fast and carries the full penalty of qada and kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled upon
The act is swallowing a grain (wheat, sesame, or similar) from outside the mouth while fasting.
The grain must come from external source, not from inside the mouth (e.g., between teeth).
2
Determine the ruling
The fast is broken and kaffarah (expiation) is required along with qada (making up the day).
This is the chosen opinion (al-mukhtar) among later Hanafis.
3
Understand the reasoning
The act is considered eating (akl), which is a clear invalidator of the fast.
The amount is insignificant; even a sesame seed suffices.
4
Note the authority level
The phrase 'fi al-mukhtar' indicates this is the preferred view for fatwa among later Hanafi authorities.
This overrides any earlier weaker opinions.
5
Recognize the practical implication
Accidental swallowing triggers the full penalty; one must make up the day and perform kaffarah (fasting 60 consecutive days or feeding 60 poor).
This strictness underscores the sanctity of the fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما لا يفسد الصوم وابتلاع حبة حنطة وابتلاع حبة سمسمة او نحوها من خارج فمه فى المختار ترجمه : (۱۰۵۳) [۱۰] اور خارج منھ سے تل یا اس کے مانند کوئی دانہ نگل لیا، مختار قول میں ۔ تشریح : اوپر گزرا کہ منہ کے باہر سے تل کے برابر کھایا تو اس سے روزہ بھی ٹوٹے گا اور کفارہ بھی لازم ہوگا، کیونکہ کھانا پایا گیا۔
Chapter on what does not break the fast. And swallowing a grain of wheat and swallowing a grain of sesame or similar from outside his mouth, in the chosen opinion. Translation: And swallowing from outside a sesame seed or similar, in the chosen opinion. Commentary: It passed above that if he ate from outside the mouth equal to a sesame seed, then his fast breaks and kaffarah becomes necessary, because food is found.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on what does not break the fast, but the first ruling actually states what does break it: swallowing even a single grain of wheat or sesame from outside the mouth. The phrase 'in the chosen opinion' (fi al-mukhtar) indicates this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school. The Urdu commentary clarifies that this action invalidates the fast and incurs both qada and kaffarah because it constitutes eating. Note that the chapter title refers to a broader discussion; this specific ruling is an exception that does break the fast.

Key Terms
مختار (mukhtar): The chosen or preferred opinion within the Hanafi school, usually from later authorities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on swallowing a single sesame seed from outside the mouth while fasting according to the chosen opinion?
A) The fast is valid and no compensation is needed.
B) The fast is broken and only qada is required.
C) The fast is broken and both qada and kaffarah are required.
D) The fast is broken but only if the seed is chewed.

Key Takeaway
Swallowing even a single grain from outside the mouth breaks the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah, as it constitutes eating.

Eating Clay While Fasting: Custom Makes It Food
Ruling on eating Armenian clay and other clay if accustomed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for Armenian clay and apply the custom-based criterion to determine when non-Armenian clay invalidates the fast.

Core Idea
What is customarily eaten breaks the fast — even clay, if habit makes it food.

This passage addresses a specific fiqh question: does eating clay break the fast? The Hanafi school distinguishes between clay that is commonly eaten (like Armenian clay) and other clay that may become food through habitual consumption. The core principle is that any substance customarily treated as food (tu'm) triggers the same rules as ordinary food when fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of clay
Separate Armenian clay (tin armani) from other clays. The ruling for each is different.
Armenian clay is treated as food by default because people eat it.
2
Apply the rule for Armenian clay
Since Armenian clay is customarily eaten, consuming it during fasting invalidates the fast absolutely, requiring qada and kaffarah.
No need to check personal habit.
3
Apply the rule for non-Armenian clay
For other clays, check if the individual is accustomed to eating it. If habitual, it is considered food and breaks the fast; otherwise not.
The habit can be personal or specific to a region.
4
Confirm the consequence
Both cases, when the fast is broken, require qada (make up the day) and kaffarah (expiation) if the act was deliberate and without excuse.
Kaffarah is like for breaking fast by eating food.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واكل الطين الارمني مطلقا والطين غير الارمني كالطفل ان اعتاد اكله ترجمه : (۱۰۵۴) [۱۱] مطلق ارمنی مٹی کھانا۔ [۱۲] اور ارمنی مٹی کے علاوہ کوئی اور مٹی کھا لینا، جیسا کہ بچے کو کھانے کی عادت پڑ جاتی ہے۔ تشریح : ارمنی مٹی کو لوگ کھاتے ہیں اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا ، اسی طرح کسی اور مٹی کھانے کی عادت پڑ گئی تو اس کے کھانے سے بھی کفارہ لازم ہوگا ، کیونکہ کھانا پایا گیا۔
And eating Armenian clay absolutely, and non-Armenian clay like dirt if he is accustomed to eating it. Translation: [11] Eating Armenian clay absolutely. [12] And eating clay other than Armenian, like when a child becomes accustomed to eating it. Commentary: People eat Armenian clay, so eating it makes kaffarah necessary; similarly, if one becomes accustomed to eating other clay, eating it also makes kaffarah necessary, because food is found.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about eating clay while fasting. It distinguishes between Armenian clay and other clays. Armenian clay is commonly eaten, so if a fasting person consumes it, the fast breaks and requires kaffarah. For non-Armenian clay (like ordinary dirt), the ruling depends on whether the person is accustomed to eating it. If he is not accustomed, it may not be considered food; but if he has the habit, then it is considered nutrition and incurs kaffarah. The reasoning is that the substance is treated as food (tu'm) due to customary consumption.

Key Terms
طين ارمني (tin armani): A type of clay imported from Armenia, traditionally eaten for medicinal or taste purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does eating non-Armenian clay break the fast according to this passage?
A) Always breaks the fast.
B) Only if eaten in a large quantity.
C) Only if the person is accustomed to eating it.
D) It never breaks the fast.

Key Takeaway
Armenian clay always breaks the fast; other clay breaks it only if customarily eaten.

Eating a Small Amount of Salt
Ruling on eating a small amount of salt in the chosen opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on eating a small amount of salt while fasting and explain why it requires kaffarah.

Core Idea
Even a little salt breaks your fast—it's a food and triggers qada and kaffarah.

This passage addresses a specific but important detail: the ruling on deliberately eating a small amount of salt while fasting. The Hanafi chosen opinion holds that any amount of salt, because it is a food substance, invalidates the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the broader principle that any consumable food or drink, no matter how small, breaks the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is being ruled upon? Eating a small amount of salt while fasting. The ruling is that it invalidates the fast.
2
Determine the Hukm Level
The act is haram (prohibited) because it breaks the fast. The consequence is that both qada and kaffarah are required.
Kaffarah in this context is fasting 60 consecutive days or feeding 60 poor people.
3
Understand the Basis
Why does salt break the fast? Because it is a food substance (tu'm). The Hanafi school considers any food or drink that enters the stomach as breaking the fast.
4
Note the Chosen Opinion
The text says 'fi al-mukhtar' meaning this is the relied-upon position within the Hanafi school, even though some scholars might differentiate based on quantity.
This is the mufta bihi view.
5
Recognize the Consequence
The consequence is that the fast becomes invalid (batil) and qada and kaffarah are obligatory.
This applies regardless of the amount, as long as the consumption is deliberate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والملح القليل في المختار ترجمه : (۱۰۵۵) [۱۳] اور مختار مسلک کے اعتبار سے قلیل نمک ( موجب کفارہ ہے ) تشریح : نمک بھی کھانے کی چیز ہے اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے بھی کفارہ لازم ہو گا۔
And a small amount of salt in the chosen opinion. Translation: [13] And according to the chosen madhhab, a little salt (incurs kaffarah). Commentary: Salt is also a food item, so eating it also makes kaffarah necessary.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This ruling states that even a small quantity of salt, if eaten deliberately while fasting, breaks the fast and requires qada and kaffarah. The phrase 'in the chosen opinion' (fi al-mukhtar) again indicates this is the preferred view. The reasoning is that salt is a food substance (tu'm), and its consumption falls under eating. Note that there may be other opinions within the Hanafi school that differentiate based on quantity, but the chosen opinion holds that any amount suffices.

Key Terms
ملح قليل (milh qalil): A small amount of salt, as opposed to a large amount that would clearly break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It does not break the fast.
B) It breaks the fast but only qada is required.
C) It breaks the fast and kaffarah is required.
D) It only breaks the fast if the salt is from food.

Key Takeaway
Even a tiny amount of salt deliberately eaten breaks the fast and demands both qada and kaffarah.

Eating Deliberately After Mistakenly Thinking the Fast Broke
Ruling on eating after mistakenly thinking the fast broke due to certain acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a person deliberately eats after mistakenly thinking his fast broke due to one of six specified actions, and identify the two exceptions that excuse from kaffarah.

Core Idea
Mistaken belief does not break the fast, but deliberate eating with intent to break does.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain actions such as backbiting or cupping do not break the fast. However, if a person mistakenly believes his fast is broken and then deliberately eats, he has intentionally broken the fast. This lesson examines the ruling and the exceptions where kaffarah is not required.

Guided Path
1
List the six actions
Identify the six actions mentioned that do not actually break the fast: backbiting, cupping, touching with desire, kissing with desire, sleeping without ejaculation, and oiling the mustache.
These actions are only triggers for the mistaken belief.
2
Understand the mistaken belief scenario
The person performs one of these actions and mistakenly believes his fast is broken. He then deliberately eats with the intention to break the fast.
The fast is broken by the deliberate eating, not by the initial action.
3
Recognize the default ruling
The default ruling is that kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory because the person intentionally broke the fast.
Kaffarah includes fasting two consecutive months or feeding 60 poor people.
4
Identify the two exceptions
Kaffarah is waived if: (1) a qualified jurist (faqih) gave a fatwa that the act breaks the fast, or (2) the person heard a hadith suggesting the fast breaks but did not know the proper interpretation (ta'wil) in the Hanafi school.
In these cases, only qada (make-up fast) is required.
5
Apply the knowledge condition
If the person knew the correct interpretation of the hadith (that the fast does not actually break) and still ate, kaffarah is due. Knowledge removes the excuse.
This emphasizes the importance of learning the school's ta'wil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واكله عمدا بعد غيبة او حجامة او مس او قبلة بشهوة او بعد مضاجعة من غير انزال او بعد دهن شاربه ظانا انه افطر بذالك الا اذا افتاه فقيه او سمع الحديث ولم يعرف تاويله على المذهب وان عرف تاويله وجبت عليه الكفارة ترجمه : (۱۰۵۷) [۱۶] اور عمداً کھا لینا غیبت کے بعد - (۱۷) پچھنا لگانے کے بعد - (۱۸) یا شہوت سے ( عورت کو ) چھو لینے کے بعد ۔ [۱۹] یا بوسہ لینے کے بعد ۔ [۲۰] یا انزال کے بغیر مضاجعت کے بعد ۔ [۲۱] یا مونچھ کو تیل لگانے کے بعد، یہ گمان کرتے ہوئے کہ ان چیزوں سے روزہ ٹوٹ گیا۔ تشریح : ان چھ مسئلوں میں روزہ ٹوٹتا نہیں ہے البتہ حدیث میں ہے کہ روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا، یعنی ٹوٹنے کے قریب ہو جائے گا، اس لئے یہ گمان کر کے کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا دوبارہ جان کر کھا لیا تو اب روزہ ٹوٹا اس لئے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ مسئلے کی تشریح یہ ہے کہ [۱۲] غیبت کی جس سے خیال ہوا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا ہے اس کے بعد جان کر کھا لیا۔ [۱۷] پچھنا لگانے کے بعد خیال ہوا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا اس لئے کھا لیا۔ [۱۸] یا شہوت سے عورت کو چھو لیا اور سمجھا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا اس کے بعد جان کر کھالیا تو کفارہ لازم ہو گا [۱۹] یا بوسہ لیا اور سمجھا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا اور پھر کھالیا تو کفارہ لازم ہو گا ۔ [۲۰] صرف انزال ہو جائے تو اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا ہے لیکن اس نے سمجھا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا، پھر جان کر کھا لیا تو کفارہ لازم ہوگا، کیونکہ اب جان کر روزہ توڑا ہے [۲۱] یا مونچھ کو تیل لگانے لگانے کے بعد، یہ گمان کیا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا اور جان کر کھا لیا تو اب روزہ توڑا ہے اس لئے کفار ولا زم ہوگا۔ ترجمه : (۱۰۵۸) مگر یہ کہ کسی فقیہ نے فتوی دیا، یا کوئی حدیث سنی اور اس کی تاویل (اپنے ) مذہب میں معلوم نہ ہو، اور اگر اس کی کوئی تاویل جانتا تھا تو اس پر کفارہ واجب ہے۔ تشریح کسی فقیہ نے عام آدمی کوفتوی دیا کہ اوپر کی چھ چیزوں سے روزہ ٹوٹ جاتا ہے اس لئے اس نے اوپر کے کام کر کے سمجھا کہ روزہ ٹوٹ گیا اس لئے پھر جان کر کھا لیا تو اس پر قضا ہے کفارہ نہیں ہے، کیونکہ وہ فقیہ کے فتوی سے معذور ہے۔ یا مثلا حدیث پڑھی کہ پچھنا لگوانے سے روزہ ٹوٹ جاتا ہے اور اس کو اس کی تاویل معلوم نہیں تھی کہ اس حدیث کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کمزوری کی وجہ سے روزہ توڑنے قریب ہو جاتا ہے، اس لئے بعد میں کھا لیا تو کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا، کیونکہ تاویل معلوم نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے وہ معذور ہے۔ ہاں اگر اس کو حدیث کی تاویل کا پتہ تھا پھر بھی کھا لیا تو اب یہ معذور نہیں ہے اس لئے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔
And he eats deliberately after backbiting, or cupping, or touching with desire, or kissing with desire, or after sleeping without ejaculation, or after oiling his mustache, thinking that he broke the fast by that. Except if a faqih gave him a fatwa, or he heard the hadith and did not know its interpretation in the madhhab; and if he knew its interpretation, then kaffarah is obligatory upon him. Translation and commentary as in the Urdu.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This is a complex ruling statement about six actions that do not actually break the fast (backbiting, cupping, touching with desire, kissing, sleeping without ejaculation, oiling the mustache). However, if a person does any of these and mistakenly believes his fast is broken, then deliberately eats, he has intentionally broken the fast and must pay kaffarah. There are two exceptions: (1) if a qualified jurist (faqih) issued a fatwa that the fast breaks due to these acts, then the person is excused and only qada is required; (2) if he heard a hadith that seems to indicate the fast breaks, but he does not know the proper interpretation (ta'wil) according to the Hanafi school, he is also excused. But if he knows the interpretation and still eats, kaffarah is due. The commentary explains each action in detail, emphasizing that the fast does not actually break from the act itself, but the subsequent deliberate eating breaks it if done with the intent to end the fast.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil): Interpretation of a hadith that reconciles it with the school's position, e.g., 'near to breaking' rather than actual breaking.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast, which is fasting two consecutive months or feeding 60 poor people.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed fast by fasting another day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone backbites while fasting, mistakenly thinks his fast is broken, then deliberately eats, what is the ruling?
A) No kaffarah because backbiting does not break the fast.
B) Kaffarah is obligatory because he intentionally ate.
C) Only qada is required.
D) The fast is valid because backbiting is not eating.

Key Takeaway
Deliberate eating after mistakenly believing the fast broke requires kaffarah, unless the person acted on a faqih's fatwa or was ignorant of the hadith's interpretation.

Kaffarah on a Woman Who Complies with a Coercer
Ruling on kaffarah for a woman who complies with coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kaffarah for a woman who willingly complies with a man coercing her to break the fast and identify the key condition that triggers liability.

Core Idea
A woman who willingly complies with coercion to break the fast must pay kaffarah — compliance nullifies the excuse of duress.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a fasting woman is coerced by a man into having intercourse, but she willingly complies. According to the ruling, she is liable for kaffarah because her active consent transfers responsibility. This highlights the Hanafi principle that outward willingness, even under duress, makes the person accountable for the prohibited act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties involved
Recognize that the act is intercourse during a fast. The parties are a man (coercer) and a woman (the one coerced). The woman is fasting.
2
Determine the woman's response to coercion
The ruling applies only if the woman willingly complies (tawa'at). This means she does not resist and internally agrees to the act. If she resists or is forced against her will, the ruling differs.
Compliance can be by action (not resisting) or by silent consent.
3
Apply the ruling
When the woman willingly complies, kaffarah becomes obligatory (wajib) upon her. The ruling emphasizes that the obligator is the woman herself, regardless of the coercion.
The man also incurs kaffarah, but that is not the focus of this passage.
4
Contrast with non-compliance
If the woman does not comply (e.g., resists physically), no kaffarah is due on her. The entire responsibility falls on the coercing man.
This distinction is critical for understanding the Hanafi position on coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتجب الكفارة على من طاوعت مكرها ترجمه : (۱۰۵۹) [۲۲] اور کفارہ واجب ہے اس عورت پر جو اتباع کرے مکرہ کی ( یعنی مرد کو جماع پر مجبور کیا اور عورت خوشی سے تیار ہوگئی)۔
And kaffarah is obligatory on the one who complied with a coercer. Commentary: Kaffarah is obligatory on that woman who follows the one who coerces (i.e., the man forced her to have intercourse and she willingly agreed).
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This ruling addresses a specific scenario: a man forces a woman to have intercourse while she is fasting, and she willingly complies (i.e., she does not resist and accepts the act). In such a case, she is liable for kaffarah because her compliance indicates intentional participation in breaking the fast. The man is also liable, but the focus here is on the woman's responsibility. The Urdu commentary clarifies that the man coerced her but she willingly agreed, so she shares the guilt. This highlights that coercion alone does not excuse her if she actively consents.

Key Terms
مكرہ (mukrih): One who coerces another into an action, here specifically a man forcing intercourse on a fasting woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a woman incur kaffarah under coercion according to this passage?
A) If she is forced and resists.
B) If she willingly complies with the coercion.
C) She never incurs kaffarah under coercion.
D) Only if the man also incurs kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is obligatory on a woman who willingly complies with a coercer to break her fast, as consent nullifies the excuse of duress.

intermediate
6 min
Putting Oil/Water in Ear Breaks Fast Without Kaffarah
Ruling on ear drops invalidating the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on putting oil or water in the ear while fasting and explain why qada is required but not kaffarah.

Core Idea
Putting oil or water in the ear breaks the fast, but no kaffarah is required.

This lesson examines a specific ruling about ear entry during fasting. While the fast becomes invalid and must be made up, the absence of kaffarah highlights that not every invalidating act incurs the full expiation. We will explore the proof from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and understand the underlying rationale based on the ear being an open passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Here, it is deliberately putting oil or water into the ear while fasting. The ruling is that the fast becomes invalid (fasid) but without kaffarah.
2
Locate the Dalil
The proof cited is the statement (athar) of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: he permitted snuff (su'ut) for the fasting person but disliked putting anything in the ears, indicating that the ear is a valid entry point for substances that break the fast.
The hadith is mursal and recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does this dalil prove the ruling? Ibrahim's permission of snuff shows that not all nasal entries break the fast, but his disapproval of ear entry implies that the ear is a passage that can invalidate the fast. The Hanafi school considers the ear as an open cavity (zahir) through which substances can reach the brain.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Other schools may not consider ear entry as nullifying unless the substance reaches the stomach or throat. The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The soundest view (al-ashahh) is that the fast is broken and requires qada but no kaffarah. This is the operative position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
او اقطر في أذنه دهنا او ماء في الاصح. عن ابراهيم قال : لا بأس بالسعوط للصائم و كره الصب في الآذان ( مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ، باب الصائم يستعط ، ج ثانی ، ص ۳۰۴ نمبر ۹۲۶۳)
Or he puts oil or water in his ear, according to the soundest view. From Ibrahim: 'There is no harm in the fasting person using snuff, but he disliked putting (anything) in the ears.'
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This is a ruling regarding oil or water dripped into the ear while fasting. According to the soundest view (al-ashahh), the fast is invalidated because the ear is considered an open passage to the brain, even though modern medicine shows the eardrum as a barrier. Shariah relies on outward appearances (zahir). Since the substance entered through an apparent hole, the fast breaks. However, no kaffarah (expiation) is required because it is not direct ingestion. The proof is the athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, who allowed snuff (su'ut) but disliked putting anything in the ears, indicating that the ear is a valid entry point.

Key Terms
اقطار (iqṭār): To put drops into (the ear)
سعوط (su‘ūṭ): Snuff; medicine inhaled through the nose
صَب (ṣabb): Pouring (into the ear)

Medicine Reaching Stomach/Brain Through Wound
Ruling with proof: medicine via wound invalidates fast, qada only

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when medicine enters the internal cavity via a wound, explain why only qada is required, and identify the proof from the athar of Ata ibn Abi Rabah.

Core Idea
Medicine entering the body through a wound invalidates the fast but does not require kaffarah.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a fasting person applies medicine to a deep wound that reaches the stomach or brain. The fast becomes invalid, but the consequence is only qada (replacement day), not kaffarah (expiation). We will analyse the ruling, its proof from the tradition of Ata, and the reasoning behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Circumstance
Recognise that the act is applying medicine to a deep wound (ja'ifah or ammah) where the medicine enters the internal cavity or brain. This is not normal ingestion.
Ja'ifah penetrates to the intestines; ammah reaches the brain membrane.
2
Determine the Ruling
The fast becomes invalid (fasid) because the medicine reaches the internal cavity. The route (wound) does not matter for nullification.
The hukm level is fasid, not batil, but the practical consequence is the same: the fast is broken.
3
Identify the Consequence
Only qada (replacing one day) is required; no kaffarah (expiation). This is because the entry is not via the normal route and is not considered intentional eating/drinking.
Kaffarah is only for deliberate, normal ingestion.
4
Examine the Dalil
The proof is an athar from Ata ibn Abi Rabah: 'He disliked that a person insert anything during Ramadan in the daytime, and if he does, let him compensate a day, but that day is not considered broken.'
The athar is mursal (from a tabi'i) but relied upon by Hanafi scholars.
5
Analyse the Legal Inference
Ata says 'let him compensate a day' (qada) and 'not considere broken' (no kaffarah). The Hanafi school uses this to distinguish between invalidating the fast (qada) and requiring expiation (kaffarah).
The phrase 'lā yuftir' is interpreted as 'no kaffarah', not 'fast remains valid'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
او داوى جائفة او آمة بدواء ووصل الى جوفه او دماغه. عن عطاء كره ان يستدخل الانسان شيئا في رمضان بالنهار فان فعل فليبدل يوما ولا يفطر ذلك اليوم (مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب الحقیة فی رمضان والرجل یصیب اہلہ ، ص۱۵۳، نمبر ۷۵۰۷ )
Or he treats a deep wound (ja'ifah) or a skull wound (ammah) with medicine and it reaches his cavity or his brain. From Ata: 'He disliked that a person insert anything during Ramadan in the daytime, and if he does, let him compensate a day, but that day is not considered broken.'
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This lesson addresses the case where a fasting person applies medicine to a deep wound that reaches the stomach (jauf) or brain. The fast is invalidated because the medicine enters the internal cavity through the wound. However, no kaffarah is required because it is not intentional ingestion of food or drink. The proof is an athar from Ata ibn Abi Rabah, who disliked inserting anything into the body during Ramadan and prescribed only qada (replacing a day), not kaffarah. Ja'ifah refers to a wound penetrating to the intestines, and ammah to a wound reaching the brain membrane.

Key Terms
جائفة (jā’ifah): A wound that reaches the internal cavity (jauf), specifically the intestines.
آمة (āmmah): A wound that reaches the brain membrane (dimagh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The fast is invalid and the day must be completed without eating.

Key Takeaway
Medicine entering the cavity via a wound invalidates the fast but only requires qada, not kaffarah.

Involuntary Swallowing of Rainwater or Snow During Fasting
Ruling on rainwater/snow entering the throat involuntarily

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on involuntary swallowing of rainwater or snow during fasting and distinguish when qada alone is required versus when kaffarah is necessary.

Core Idea
Involuntary swallowing of rain/snow breaks the fast but requires only qada, not kaffarah.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a fasting person finds rainwater or snow entering their throat without deliberate action. The ruling clarifies that the fast is invalidated because the substance enters the stomach, but the absence of intent means no expiation (kaffarah) is due—only make-up (qada). Understanding the boundary between voluntary and involuntary is critical for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The act is swallowing rainwater or snow during fasting. The ruling: the fast becomes fasid (invalid).
2
Determine the key condition
The crucial condition is that the swallowing is involuntary ('lam yabtali'hu bi sun'ihi').
3
Contrast with intentional swallowing
If voluntary, the ruling changes: kaffarah is required. Understand the difference.
4
Apply the consequence
For involuntary: qada only. For voluntary: qada and kaffarah.

Source Text

Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This ruling addresses when rainwater or snow enters the throat without the person's deliberate action. According to the soundest Hanafi view, the fast is invalidated because the substance enters the stomach through the mouth. However, since the person did not intentionally swallow it, no kaffarah is required—only qada. The condition 'lam yabtala'hu bi sun'ihi' (he did not swallow it by his own action) is crucial; if he deliberately swallows collected water, it would require kaffarah.

Key Terms

Mistaken or Coerced Breaking of Fast
Rulings on fast broken by mistake or coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a fast broken by mistake or coercion and explain why kaffarah is not required.

Core Idea
A fast is invalidated by anything entering the body, even unintentionally or under duress, but kaffarah is only for deliberate voluntary violations.

In this lesson, we examine scenarios where the fast is broken without full volition: accidental swallowing of mouth-rinse water, or being forced to break the fast, even by intercourse. The key is that the fast is nullified because something entered the body, but the penalty of kaffarah is not incurred due to the absence of deliberate intent. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of qada and kaffarah in real situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
Read the passage and list the three cases mentioned: accidental swallowing of water from rinsing the mouth, being coerced to break the fast (general), and a woman being forced into intercourse.
The text treats these as similar in ruling.
2
Determine the legal consequence
For each scenario, state whether the fast is valid or invalid. According to the passage, the fast becomes invalid (fasid) because something entered the body.
Invalidation occurs regardless of intent.
3
Distinguish qada from kaffarah
Identify what expiation is required. The passage implies that only qada (make-up day) is required, not kaffarah (expiation). Explain that kaffarah is only for deliberate, voluntary violations.
This is based on the rational principle (dalil aqli) that kaffarah requires willful disobedience.
4
Note the disagreement
Some other schools hold that if the coerced person had any choice (e.g., they could have endured harm), kaffarah may be required. The Hanafi position is that coercion eliminates full liability.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Or he breaks his fast by mistake due to miswak water reaching his cavity, or he breaks it under coercion, even by intercourse, or a woman is forced into intercourse.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This lesson covers cases where the fast is broken unintentionally or under duress. Examples: water from rinsing the mouth (madmadah) reaches the throat by accident; a person is forced to break the fast, even by means of sexual intercourse; a woman is coerced into intercourse. In all these, the fast is invalidated because something entered the body, but no kaffarah is required because the act was not voluntary. The person must only make up the day (qada). The principle is that kaffarah is only for deliberate, volitional violation of the fast. Coercion eliminates liability for kaffarah.

Key Terms

ماء المضمضة (mā’ al-madmadah): Water used for rinsing the mouth during wudu

Woman Breaking Fast Due to Fear of Illness from Hard Labor
Ruling on a woman breaking fast due to fear of illness from domestic labor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a woman breaking fast out of fear of falling ill from domestic service, identify the analogy (qiyas) to pregnant and nursing women, and explain why only qada is required without kaffarah.

Core Idea
Fear of illness from hard labor permits breaking fast with only qada, by analogy to the concession for pregnant and nursing women.

Imagine a slave woman or a wife who must perform heavy household chores while fasting. She genuinely fears that continuing the fast will make her ill. Can she break her fast, and if so, what is required? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: she may break the fast, and only making up the day (qada) is needed—no expiation (kaffarah). The proof comes from a Prophetic hadith granting relief to pregnant and breastfeeding women, extended by legal analogy (qiyas) to this similar situation of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the woman breaks her fast due to fear of illness from service. The Hanafi school rules that this is permissible (rukhsah) and the fast becomes fasid (invalid), requiring only qada, not kaffarah.
The ruling distinguishes between intentional breaking without excuse (requires kaffarah) and breaking due to genuine necessity.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith in which the Prophet ﷺ said that Allah has removed the obligation of fasting from the pregnant or breastfeeding woman. This demonstrates a concession for those in a state of hardship.
The hadith is reported in Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud, with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase 'al-hamil aw al-murdi' (pregnant or nursing) indicates a concession. The underlying cause (illa) is the fear of harm to oneself or the baby. Here, the woman's fear of illness from service is analogous.
Hanafi usul: the concession is generalized by qiyas when the illa is found in another case.
4
Apply the qiyas
The pregnant/nursing woman is excused from kaffarah based on genuine fear. Similarly, the woman fearing illness from service is excused. The common illa is darurah (necessity) or genuine fear of harm.
If the fear is not genuine (e.g., mild fatigue), the ruling would differ.
5
Determine the consequence
The consequence is that breaking the fast is permissible, but the day must be made up later (qada). No kaffarah is required because the dispensation aligns with the principle of removing hardship.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
او افطرت خوفا على نفسها من ان تمرض من الخدمة امة كانت او منكوحة. وعن انس بن مالك رجل من بني عبد الله بن كعب ... احدثك عن الصوم او الصيام ان الله وضع عن المسافر شطر الصلوة وعن الحامل او المرضع الصوم او الصيام قالهما النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كليهما او احدهما. Commentary
This lesson addresses a woman (slave or wife) who fears that continuing her fast while performing harsh domestic service will make her ill. In such a situation, she may break the fast, and only qada is required—no kaffarah. The proof is by analogy (qiyas) to the pregnant and breastfeeding woman, for whom the Prophet ﷺ granted the concession to break the fast and make it up later. The hadith of Anas is cited to support that Allah has relieved the pregnant and nursing of fasting (in certain situations). Since the fear of illness from service is similar to the fear of harm to oneself, the same ruling applies: break the fast, then make it up later without expiation. The principle is that necessity (darurah) removes the obligation of kaffarah.

Key Terms
خدمة (khidmah): Hard domestic labor or service that may cause illness if continued while fasting.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed fast after Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Water Poured into Sleeping Person's Mouth – Ruling on Fasting
Ruling on breaking fast during sleep by external action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when water is poured into a sleeping fasting person's mouth, explain why only qada is required and not kaffarah, and identify the key principle of intentionality in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A sleeping fasting person's fast is broken if water enters the stomach, but since there is no intent, only qada is required, not kaffarah.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a third party pours water into the mouth of a sleeping fasting person, and the water reaches the stomach. While the fast is invalidated because water entered the body, the sleeping person bears no sin or kaffarah. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that kaffarah is contingent upon deliberate violation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
Read the source text: a sleeping person has water poured into their mouth. Determine the legal effect on the fast.
The act is external (pouring) but leads to water entering the stomach.
2
Understand the Role of Intention
Analyze why kaffarah is not required. Kaffarah is only for deliberate, intentional violations (in this case, the sleeper had no control).
Intention (niyyah) is crucial for sin and heavy obligations.
3
Distinguish Between Qada and Kaffarah
Qada is making up the fast on another day; kaffarah is an extra expiation. Here, only qada is needed because the fast was broken involuntarily.
Qada is obligatory for any invalidated fast; kaffarah is extra for deliberate violations.
4
Note Cross-Madhhab Differences
Some schools may argue that the sleeping person could have taken precautions, leading to a different ruling. Understand the Hanafi response.
Hanafi emphasizes the absence of volition.

This ruling deals with a third party pouring water into the mouth of a sleeping fasting person. If the water reaches the stomach, the fast is invalidated because it entered the body. However, since the sleeping person had no intention or control over the act, no kaffarah is required. The sleeping person must make up that day (qada). This is consistent with the principle that kaffarah is contingent upon intention and voluntary action.

Key Terms

Inserting Cotton into Private Parts Invalidates Fast
Ruling with proof on insertion invalidating fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inserting an object into the anus or vagina during fasting and explain the proof from the athar and the saying of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
Intentional entry into a body cavity invalidates the fast, but no kaffarah is required.

Many people assume that only eating and drinking break the fast. The Hanafi school, however, rules that any intentional insertion of a substance into the body cavity—such as cotton into the anus or vagina—invalidates the fast. This lesson examines the textual proofs (athar and qawl sahabi) and the ruling's practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The passage describes a man inserting cotton into his anus or a woman into her vagina until it disappears. The ruling is that the fast becomes invalid (mufsid).
2
Locate the Dalil
Two proofs are cited: (1) an athar from 'Ata in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, and (2) the saying of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah in Sahih Bukhari: 'Fasting is from what enters, not from what exits.'
Both proofs are considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The athar states that if someone inserts something during Ramadan, he should replace a day and 'that day does not break his fast' – but the phrase 'la yufṭiru' indicates the fast is not valid, i.e., it breaks. The qawl sahabi directly states that fasting is affected by what enters the body.
The ruling is derived from the general principle that any entry into the body cavity breaks the fast.
4
Identify the Consequence: No Kaffarah
The passage implies that although the fast breaks, no kaffarah (expiation) is required because this act is not eating or drinking in the conventional sense. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that kaffarah is only for intentional eating, drinking, or sexual intercourse.
The source text does not explicitly state 'no kaffarah' but the context and general fiqh principles confirm it.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Some schools may hold that only insertion of a wet object breaks the fast, or that a dry object does not. The Hanafi view is that any intentional insertion (wet or dry) into the body cavity invalidates the fast.
The proof from Ibn Abbas is general: 'what enters' includes all substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یا (مرد نے) اپنے دبر میں یا (عورت نے) اپنی شرمگاہ کے اندرون میں روئی داخل کی اور اسے غائب کر دیا۔ تشریح: مرد نے اپنے پاخانے کے راستے میں روئی اندر ڈال دیا کہ وہ نظروں سے غائب ہوگئی تو گویا کہ جسم کے اندر کوئی چیز داخل ہوئی، اسی طرح عورت نے اپنی شرمگاہ میں روئی اتنا اندر داخل کر دیا کہ وہ نظروں سے غائب ہوگئی تو گویا کہ جسم کے اندر کوئی چیز داخل کی اس لئے روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔ وجه: (۱) اس اثر میں ہے۔ عن عطاء کرہ ان یستدخل الانسان شيئا في رمضان بالنهار فان فعل فليبدل يوما ولا يفطر ذلك اليوم (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الحقیة فی رمضان والرجل یصیب اہلہ ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۷۵۰۷) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کوئی چیز بدن میں داخل ہوئی تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا، یہاں فرج داخل کے اندر اور دبر کے اندر روئی داخل کی گئی اس لئے روزہ ٹوٹے گا۔ (۲) اس قول صحابی سے بھی استدلال کیا جاسکتا ہے۔ قال ابن عباس و عكرمة الصوم مما دخل وليس مما خرج (بخاری شریف، باب الحجامة والقين للصائم ص ۲۶۰ نمبر ۱۹۳۸) کہ جسم میں کوئی چیز داخل ہونے سے روزہ ٹوٹا۔
Or (a man) inserted cotton into his anus or (a woman) into her vagina and it disappeared. Explanation: A man inserted cotton into his stool passage such that it disappeared from sight, so it is as if something entered the body. Similarly, a woman inserted cotton into her vagina so deep that it disappeared from sight, so the fast is broken. Proof: (1) In this athar: 'Ata disliked a person inserting something during the day in Ramadan. If he does so, let him replace a day and that day does not break his fast.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) This shows that if something enters the body, the fast breaks. (2) The saying of Ibn Abbas: 'Fasting is from what enters, not from what exits.' (Sahih Bukhari) So the fast is broken by insertion.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that inserting cotton into the anus or vagina invalidates the fast. The author explains that the fast breaks because something was deliberately introduced into the body cavity. The proof is from the athar of Ata and the saying of Ibn Abbas, both indicating that the fast is broken by entry of a substance into the body. Note that no kaffarah (expiation) is required because this is not eating or drinking in the conventional sense.

Key Terms
دبر (dubur): The anus or rectum.
فرج (farj): The female genitalia/vagina.
اثر (athar): A report from a Companion or early scholar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on inserting a dry cotton ball into the vagina during fasting?
A) The fast is valid.
B) The fast is invalid and kaffarah is required.
C) The fast is invalid but no kaffarah is required.
D) It depends on the intention.

Key Takeaway
Intentional insertion of any substance into the anus or vagina during fasting invalidates the fast and requires qada, but not kaffarah.

Tasting and Chewing While Fasting: Ruling and Proof
States the ruling and proof for tasting and chewing without excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tasting and chewing food while fasting and identify the condition that permits these actions.

Core Idea
Tasting or chewing without swallowing is permitted in need; otherwise it is disliked.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text addresses a common practical question for a fasting person: can one taste or chew food without breaking the fast? The author presents the ruling as part of a list of disliked acts, then provides a proof from the tabi'i Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that clarifies the boundary between permisibility and prohibition. Understanding this ruling helps avoid unnecessary sin while also preserving the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the explicit mention of tasting (dhawq) and chewing (madgh) without excuse. The text states that these are among seven disliked things for the fasting person.
The ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked, not sinful) when without necessity.
2
Recognize the condition for permisibility
The commentary explains that if there is a necessity (e.g., tasting for seasoning or chewing for a baby), the act becomes permissible (ja'iz), provided nothing enters the throat.
Necessity is defined by custom; if no necessity, the original ruling of karahah applies.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The text cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'There is no harm if a woman chews food for her child while fasting, as long as it does not enter her throat.'
This is a qawl tabi'i, considered a proof in Hanafi usul when not contradicted by stronger evidence.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لا بأس' (there is no harm) indicates permisibility (ibahah). The conditional 'as long as it does not enter her throat' implies that if it enters the throat, the fast breaks.
Thus, the fast remains valid if nothing reaches the stomach; the action is not inherently invalidating.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
Some other schools may consider any tasting without excuse as invalidating the fast. The Hanafis distinguish based on the presence of necessity and the condition of not swallowing.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a Hanafi may taste food in need, while a Shafi'i might require expiation if done intentionally.

[2] And chewing. And if there is no necessity, then tasting or chewing is makruh for the fasting person. Proof: (1) It is narrated from Ibrahim: 'There is no harm if a woman chews food for her child while she is fasting, as long as it does not enter her throat.' Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the section on disliked acts for the fasting person. It first lists seven disliked things, then details the first: tasting and chewing without excuse. The ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) to taste or chew food without a valid excuse. If there is a necessity (e.g., tasting for seasoning or chewing for a child), it becomes permissible (ja'iz), provided that nothing enters the throat. If something reaches the stomach, the fast is broken. The proof is the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i (a tabi'i) permitting a woman to chew for her child as long as it does not go down her throat. This is recorded in the Musannafs of Ibn Abi Shayba and Abd al-Razzaq. The Hanafi school considers this as evidence that such actions are permissible in need but otherwise disliked.

Key Terms
ذوق (dhawq): Tasting, i.e., placing food on the tongue to test it, without swallowing.
مضغ (madgh): Chewing, i.e., breaking food with teeth without swallowing.
علک (‘ilk): A type of gum used for cleaning teeth, chewed but not swallowed.
كره (kariha): Disliked in the sense of makruh tanzihi (lesser dislike), not sinful but better to avoid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram and breaks the fast.
B) It is makruh and breaks the fast if any enters the throat.
C) It is permissible if she spits it out and nothing goes down.
D) It is wajib to avoid tasting.

Key Takeaway
Tasting or chewing without swallowing is makruh without necessity, but permissible with need provided nothing reaches the throat; the fast remains valid.

The Hanafi Ruling on Chewing Gum While Fasting
Ruling with proof on chewing gum while fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on chewing gum while fasting and apply the condition that distinguishes a valid fast from a broken fast.

Core Idea
Chewing gum does not break the fast as long as nothing reaches the stomach, but it is disliked without necessity.

Fasting Muslims often wonder about chewing gum: does it break the fast? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on whether any substance enters the stomach. This lesson examines the evidence from the early generations (salaf) and derives the operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is chewing 'ilk (gum). The ruling has two parts: if nothing enters the throat, the fast is valid but the chewing is makruh tanzih; if anything enters the stomach, the fast breaks.
The ruling distinguishes between intention and outcome.
2
Locate the dalil
Two athar are cited: one from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that he allowed chewing as long as nothing enters the throat; another from Umm Habiba that she disliked it for the fasting person.
These are qawl sahabi (or tabi'i) proofs.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The first athar uses 'rakhkhas' (permitted) indicating permissibility; the second uses 'karihat' (disliked) indicating it is better to avoid. Together they establish makruh tanzih.
The Hanafi usul principle: a permission (rukhsa) does not imply recommendation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and tarjih
Some scholars may hold that any chewing invalidates the fast due to the possibility of small particles entering the throat. The Hanafi position relies on the explicit condition in the athar and is the mufta bihi.
The principle: certainty (no entry) overrides doubt (possibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (١٠٩٩)[٣] اور علک کا چبانا۔
تشریح : علک ایک تم کا گوند ہے جو دانت صاف کرنے کے لئے عورتیں چباتی ہیں۔ اس لئے اگر صرف دانت صاف کرنے
كے لئے چبا کر پھینک دیا اور حلق میں اس کا دانہ نہیں گیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ کیونکہ پیٹ میں کوئی چیز نہیں گئی لیکن ایسا کرنا مکروہ ہے
وجه : (١) اثر میں ہے عن ابراهيم انه رخص فى مضغ العلك للصائم مالم يدخله حلقه (مصنف ابن ابی شبيبة ، ٣١ من رخص في مضغ العلك للصائم ، ج جلد ثانی، ص ٢٩٧، نمبر ۹۱۷۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ علک چبانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا بشرطیکہ کوئی چیز حلق میں نہ جائے (٢) بغیر ضرورت کے چبانا مکروہ ہے اس کی دلیل یہ قول تابعی ہے . عن ام حبيبة زوج النبي الا الله أنها كرهت مضغ العلك للصائم ۔ (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ، ٣٢ من كره مضغ العلك للصائم ، ج جلد ثانی ص ٢٩٨ نمبر ۹۱۸٦ سفن بیہقی ، باب من کرد مضغ العلك للصائم، ج رابع ، ص ٤٤٧، نمبر ۸۳۰٦) اس اثر میں ہے کہ روزہ دار کے لئے علک چبانا مکروہ ہے۔
اصول : یہی ہے کہ پیٹ میں گوند پہونچے تو روزہ ٹوٹے گا ورنہ نہیں۔
لغت : مفغ : چبانا ۔ العلک : چبانے کا گوند۔
Translation: (1099) [3] And chewing 'ilk. Commentary: 'Ilk is a type of gum that women chew for cleaning teeth. Therefore, if one chews only for cleaning teeth and spits it out, and no particle goes into the throat, the fast will not break because nothing entered the stomach, but doing so is makruh. Proof: (1) It is narrated from Ibrahim that he allowed chewing 'ilk for the fasting person as long as it does not enter his throat. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 31: Those who allowed chewing 'ilk for the fasting person, vol. 2, p. 297, no. 9179) From this athar it is understood that chewing 'ilk does not break the fast provided that nothing enters the throat. (2) Without necessity, it is makruh; its evidence is the saying of the tabi'i: From Umm Habiba, wife of the Prophet (may Allah be pleased with her), that she disliked chewing 'ilk for the fasting person. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 32: Those who disliked chewing 'ilk for the fasting person, vol. 2, p. 298, no. 9186; also Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter: Those who disliked chewing 'ilk for the fasting person, vol. 4, p. 447, no. 8306) This athar indicates that chewing 'ilk is makruh for the fasting person. Principle: If gum reaches the stomach, the fast breaks; otherwise not. Lexicon: madgh = chewing; al-‘ilk = chewing gum.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding chewing 'ilk (a type of gum) while fasting. The ruling is that if one chews 'ilk and spits it out without any part entering the throat, the fast remains valid, but the action is makruh (disliked) if done without necessity. The proof comes from two athar: one from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i permitting it as long as nothing goes down, and another from Umm Habiba (may Allah be pleased with her) indicating dislike. The principle is that if any substance reaches the stomach, the fast is broken; otherwise, the fast is sound. This is consistent with the Hanafi school's approach that external things entering the stomach invalidate the fast, but merely chewing with spitting does not.

Key Terms
علک (‘ilk): A chewing gum used for cleaning teeth, derived from the pistachio tree.
رخص (rakhkhas): He permitted, indicating a concession (rukhsa) rather than a strict ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is still valid because it was accidental.
B) The fast breaks because something entered the stomach.
C) It is makruh but the fast remains valid.
D) The fast becomes void only if a large piece is swallowed.

Key Takeaway
Chewing gum while fasting is valid (fast not broken) as long as nothing reaches the stomach, but it is disliked (makruh tanzih) if done without necessity.

Ruling on Kissing and Embracing While Fasting
States the Hanafi ruling on kissing/embracing while fasting with two hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing and embracing while fasting and apply the condition of fear of arousal to determine whether it is makruh or permissible.

Core Idea
Kissing while fasting is permissible for the self-controlled, but makruh if it risks breaking the fast.

This passage addresses a common question: can a fasting person kiss or embrace his spouse? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on the individual's self-control. By studying the proofs from the Prophet's actions and directives, we learn that the ruling is not absolute but conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kissing (qabla) and embracing (mubashara) while fasting. The passage states it is makruh if one fears ejaculation or intercourse; otherwise it is permissible.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith are cited: from Aisha (the Prophet kissed while fasting) and from Abu Huraira (the Prophet permitted an old man but forbade a young man).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Aisha's hadith shows permissibility, but the clause 'he was the most able to control his desire' indicates it is for those with strong self-control. Abu Huraira's hadith directly distinguishes based on age/risk.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may consider kissing absolutely prohibited, but Hanafis use the condition of risk.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi is the conditional ruling: young or easily-aroused persons should avoid (makruh); old or self-controlled persons may do so without dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (١١٠٠)[٤] بوسہ دینا۔ [٥] اور بیوی سے لپٹنا، اگر ان دونوں میں اپنی ذات پر انزال ہونے کا یا جماع کر ڈالنے سے امن نہ ہو تو مکروہ ہے۔ ظاہر روایت میں۔
تشریح : اگر اس بات کا خطرہ ہو کہ بیوی کو بوسہ دینے سے یا لپٹنے سے انزال ہو جائے گا، یا آگے جماع کر ڈالے گا تو بوسہ لینا اور لپٹنا مکروہ ہے، لیکن اگر اس کا خطرہ نہیں ہے، مثلا آدمی بوڑھا ہے تو بوسہ لینے ، یا لپٹ جانے کی گنجائش ہے۔
وجه: (ا) عن عائشة قالت كان النبى الله يقبل ويباشر و هو صائم و كان أملككم لاربه (بخارى شريف، باب المباشر للصائم ، ص ٣٠٩، نمبر ۱۹۲۷) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور نے روزے کی حالت میں بوسہ لیا ہے۔ (٢) حدیث میں
هے۔ عن ابی ھریرة ان رجلا سال النبي الله عن المباشرة للصائم فرخص له واتاه آخر فنهاه فاذا الذي رخص له شيخ والذى نهاه شاب ۔ (ابوداؤد شریف، باب کراہیة للشاب ص ٣٣١ نمبر ٧ ٢٣٨) حدیث میں جو ان کو روکنے کی وجہ یہی تھی کہ اس کو نفس پر قابو نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے مکر وہ ہوگا۔
لغت: مردعورت کی شرمگاہ میں داخل نہ کرے صرف اوپر اوپر سے ملائے تو اس کو مباشرت فاحشہ کہتے ہیں ۔ قبل: بوسہ لینا۔
Translation: (1100) [4] Kissing. [5] And embracing one's wife if one is not safe from ejaculation or committing intercourse in apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya). Commentary: If there is fear that kissing or embracing will lead to ejaculation or further intercourse, then it is makruh. But if there is no such fear, e.g., the man is old, then there is leeway for kissing and embracing. Proof: (1) From Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss and embrace while fasting, and he was the most able of you to control his desire. (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter: Embracing the fasting person, p. 309, no. 1927) (2) From Abu Huraira: A man asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about embracing while fasting, and he permitted him. Another man came and he forbade him. The one he permitted was an old man, and the one he forbade was a young man. (Sunan Abi Dawud, chapter: Dislike for the young, p. 331, no. 238?) Lexicon: If a man touches his wife without penetration, it is called direct contact (mubasharat fahisha). Qabala: kissing.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding kissing and embracing (mubashara) while fasting. The ruling, according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya), is that these acts are makruh if one fears ejaculation or being led to intercourse. If one is safe (e.g., due to old age or strong self-control), they are permissible. The proof comes from two hadith: (1) Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) reports that the Prophet (peace be upon him) would kiss and embrace while fasting, indicating permissibility for one with control. (2) Abu Huraira narrates that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted an old man to embrace but forbade a young man, showing that the ruling depends on the risk of losing self-control. Thus, the Hanafi school makes the ruling conditional on the individual's ability to refrain from breaking the fast.

Key Terms
قبلة (qabla): Kissing, specifically on the mouth or face, while fasting.
مباشرة (mubashara): Direct physical contact between spouses without intercourse, such as touching or embracing.
انزال (inzal): Ejaculation, which invalidates the fast if intentional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A young man who fears he may ejaculate if he kisses his wife while fasting. What is the ruling?
A) Kissing is permissible for everyone.
B) It is makruh for him because of the risk.
C) It is haram and breaks the fast.
D) Only intercourse is prohibited, kissing is always allowed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on kissing while fasting is conditional: permissible for those with self-control, makruh for those at risk of breaking the fast.

Cupping and Bloodletting for the Fasting Person
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cupping (hijamah) and bloodletting (fasd) for a fasting person, identify the condition that changes the ruling, and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Cupping does not break the fast unless weakness is feared.

A common question during Ramadan is whether medical procedures that draw blood, such as cupping or blood tests, invalidate the fast. The Hanafi school, relying on the Prophet's own practice, holds that these actions are not inherently disliked or prohibited. However, if there is a genuine risk of weakness, they may become disliked. In this lesson, we examine the textual evidence and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The passage states that cupping (hijamah) and bloodletting (fasd) are not disliked (makruh) for the fasting person. Note that the default is permissibility, not prohibition.
The ruling is conditioned on the absence of fear of weakness.
2
Locate the condition that alters the ruling
The Urdu commentary specifies that if there is fear of becoming weak, then cupping becomes makruh (disliked). This is a conditional ruling based on the potential harm to the fasting person.
This condition reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding harm (darar).
3
Examine the hadith proof
The passage cites the hadith from Ibn Abbas: 'The Prophet ﷺ had cupping done while he was fasting.' This shows that the Prophet himself performed cupping while fasting, indicating its permissibility.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari (no. 1939) and is considered a sound (sahih) narration.
4
Understand the Hanafi legal reasoning
The Hanafi school derives from this action (fi'l) that cupping is permissible and does not break the fast. The absence of a prohibition or command to break the fast confirms the default ruling.
Other schools may interpret the hadith differently, leading to ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
By analogy (qiyas), bloodletting (fasd) and other procedures that draw blood, such as modern blood tests, fall under the same ruling: permissible unless weakness is feared.
The same condition applies: if the procedure causes significant weakness, it becomes disliked.

Source Text

(1105) And cupping and bloodletting. (1106) And the miswak at the end of the day, rather it is sunnah like the beginning, even if it is wet, or for the fasting person to apply oil so that the dust of the fasting person leaves him. Commentary
This passage provides a ruling from the Hanafi school: cupping (hijamah) and bloodletting (fasd) are not disliked (makruh) for a fasting person as long as there is no risk of becoming weak. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas indicating that the Prophet Muhammad performed cupping while fasting. The text also mentions that applying oil is recommended to remove the appearance of dust, but this is secondary. The Urdu commentary clarifies that if weakness is feared, it would be disliked. This follows the Hanafi position that such actions do not break the fast and are not detested.

Key Terms
حجامة (hijamah): Cupping: a medical procedure involving drawing blood to the surface, considered not disliked for the fasting person in Hanafi fiqh.
فصد (fasd): Bloodletting: opening a vein to release blood, similar ruling to cupping.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked: an act that is not prohibited but discouraged; here it becomes makruh if weakness is feared.

Cupping and similar blood-drawing procedures are permissible for the fasting person and do not break the fast, provided there is no fear of weakness.

The Concession to Break the Fast for Fear of Harm
State ruling and proofs for breaking fast due to illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for when it is permissible to break the fast due to illness and identify the four scenarios mentioned in the text.

Core Idea
Fear of harm—whether to life, limb, or recovery time—justifies breaking the fast; ease is intended, not hardship.

In this passage, we explore the Hanafi position on breaking the fast due to illness or fear of worsening health. The text presents four graded scenarios and supports the ruling with a Quranic verse and prophetic practice. Understanding these scenarios helps you apply the concession correctly in real-life situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the four cases where breaking the fast is permissible: fear of death, loss of a limb, worsening illness, and delay in recovery.
These are graded by severity from life-threatening to mere delay.
2
Determine the ruling for each
The ruling is ja'iz (permissible) to not fast; if harm is certain, it becomes makruh to fast.
3
Locate the primary proof
The Quranic verse 2:185 explicitly links illness and travel to the concession to break and make up.
The phrase 'يريد الله بكم اليسر' indicates that ease is intended.
4
Analyze the secondary proofs
The hadith of the Prophet breaking fast in travel despite no death risk, and the hadith for elderly and breastfeeding women, show that hardship alone suffices.
The fourth hadith emphasizes that fasting is not piety when it causes harm like fainting.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi principle
Any genuine fear of harm based on experience, dominant thought, or expert Muslim physician advice justifies the concession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في عوارض الصوم
فصل : في العوارض
(۱۱۰۹ ) لمن خاف زيادة المريض او بطء البرء ترجمه : (۱۱۰۹) جسے بیماری کے بڑھ جانے کا خوف ہو یا صحت کے مؤخر ہونے کا تو اس کے لئے جائز ہے کہ روزہ نہ رکھے تشریح : یہاں چار صورتیں ہیں [1] ایسی بیماری لاحق ہے کہ روزہ رکھنے سے جان جانے کا خطرہ ہے۔[۲] عضو کے ضائع ہونے کا خطرہ ہے [۳] بیماری بڑھ جانے کا خطرہ ہے،مثلا روزہ رکھے گا تو شوگر کی بیماری بڑھ جانے کا خطرہ ہے [۴] یا بیماری تو نہیں بڑھے گی لیکن خطرہ ہے کہ روزہ رکھنے سے جو صحت دس روز میں ٹھیک ہوتی اس کے ٹھیک ہونے میں پندرہ دن لگ جائیں گے تو اس آدمی کے لئے گنجائش ہے کہ روز وا بھی نہ رکھے اور بعد میں قضا کرے۔
وجه : (۱) یہ آیت ہے۔ فـمـن شهـد مـنـكـم الشهر فليصمه ومن كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر يريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر - آیت ۱۸۵ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ مرض ہو یا سفر ہو تو روزہ توڑے گا اور دوسرے دنوں میں اس کی قضا کرے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس أن رسول الله الله خرج الى مكة في رمضان فصام فلما بلغ الكديد أفطر فأفطر الناس . ( بخاری شریف، باب اذا صام آیا ما من رمضان ثم سافر بص ۱۳۱۲، نمبر ۱۹۴۴/ ابوداود شریف، باب التاجر يفطر ، ص ۱۳۴۹، نمبر ۲۴۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ سفر میں روزہ توڑ دیا ، حالانکہ ہلاکت کا خطرہ نہیں ہوتا ہے صرف پریشانی ہوتی ہے پھر بھی روزہ توڑنے کی گنجائش ہے۔ (۳) عن ابن عباس و على الذين يطيقونه فدية طعام مسكين قال : كانت رخصة للشيخ الكبير و المرأة الكبيرة و هما يطيقان الصيام أن يفطرا و يـطـعـما مكان كل يوم مسكينا و الحبلى و المرضع اذا خافتا ۔ (ابوداود شریف، باب من قال هی منبه الشيخ والحبل ص ۱۳۸، نمبر ۲۳۱۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ بوڑھے یا دودھ پلانے والی عورت کو پریشانی ہو تو روزہ تو ڑسکتی ہے، چاہے جان کی ہلاکت کا خطرہ نہ ہو۔ (۴) اور ہلاکت کا خطرہ ہو تو روزہ رکھنا اچھا نہیں ہے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔عــن جـابـر بـن عبد الله : ان النبي له رأى رجلا يظلل عليه و الزحام عليه ، فقال ليس من البر الصيام في السفر ۔ (ابوداود شریف، باب اختیار الفطر ، ص ۱۳۴۹، نمبر ۲۴۰۷ / مسلم شریف ، باب جواز الصوم في شهر رمضان للمسافرص ۳۵۶ نمبر ۲۶۱۲/۱۱۱۵) اس حدیث میں ہے سفر میں روزہ رکھا جس سے بیہوش ہو گیا تو آپ نے فرمایا کہ سفر میں روزہ رکھنا نیکی نہیں ہے۔
Chapter on the Excuses for Fasting. Section: On the excuses. (1109) For one who fears increase of illness or slowness of recovery. Translation: (1109) For one who fears increase of illness or delay in recovery, it is permissible for him not to fast. Explanation: There are four scenarios: [1] There is an illness such that fasting would risk death. [2] Risk of losing a limb. [3] Risk of illness worsening, e.g., if fasting, there is risk of diabetes worsening. [4] Or illness will not worsen, but there is risk that recovery which would normally take ten days would take fifteen days. So such a person has the concession to not fast and to make it up later. Proof: (1) This verse: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast; and whoever is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days. Allah intends ease for you, not hardship' - Quran 2:185. From the verse, it is understood that if one is ill or traveling, he should break the fast and make it up on other days. (2) Hadith: Ibn Abbas narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) went to Mecca in Ramadan and fasted, but when he reached al-Kadid, he broke his fast and the people broke their fast. (Bukhari, Abu Dawud) This hadith shows that he broke the fast during travel even though there was no risk of death, only hardship; still there is concession to break. (3) Also from Ibn Abbas: 'And upon those who are able to fast, a ransom of feeding a poor person.' He said: It was a concession for the old man and old woman who are able to fast, that they may break and feed a poor person for each day, and the pregnant and breastfeeding if they fear. (Abu Dawud) This hadith shows that old people or breastfeeding woman can break due to hardship even if no risk of death. (4) And if there is risk of death, then it is not good to fast; evidence is the hadith of Jabir ibn Abdullah: The Prophet (peace be upon him) saw a man who was being shaded and crowded, and said: 'It is not piety to fast while traveling.' (Abu Dawud, Muslim) This hadith shows that fasting in travel caused fainting, so the Prophet said fasting in travel is not piety.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on breaking the fast due to illness or fear of worsening health. The text lists four scenarios graded by severity: fear of death, loss of a limb, worsening of illness, and delay in recovery. In all these cases, it is permissible to break the fast and make it up later. The proofs are drawn from the Quran (2:185) which explicitly allows the sick and traveler to break and make up, and from multiple hadiths that show the Prophet himself broke his fast during travel despite only hardship, and that the concession extends even to those who simply find difficulty, such as the elderly and breastfeeding women, even without immediate danger. The fourth proof highlights that when actual harm occurs (like fainting), it is not considered piety to fast. Thus, the Hanafi position is that any genuine fear of harm—whether to life, limb, or recovery time—justifies breaking the fast. The ruling is a concession (rukhsah), not an obligation, but when harm is certain, it becomes makruh to fast.

Key Terms
خوف (khawf): A state of fear based on a dominant thought (ghalabat al-zann), experience, or the statement of a Muslim expert physician.
زیادة المرض (ziyadat al-marad): Increase or worsening of an existing illness due to fasting.
بطء البرء (but' al-bur'): Slowness of recovery or delay in regaining health.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a valid reason to break the fast?
A) Fear of losing a limb
B) Fear of worsening diabetes
C) Fear of a ten-day delay in recovery becoming fifteen days
D) Fear of mild hunger

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits breaking the fast for any genuine fear of harm, including worsening illness or delayed recovery, based on the Quranic principle of ease.

Ruling for Pregnant and Breastfeeding Women in Fasting
State ruling and proofs for breaking fast due to pregnancy or breastfeeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women regarding breaking the fast and identify the proofs from Quran, Hadith, and the saying of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break the fast upon genuine fear of harm and only make up later, no fidyah.

In the Hanafi school, pregnancy and breastfeeding are recognized as valid excuses to break the fast in Ramadan when there is genuine fear of harm to mother or child. This lesson unpacks the four scenarios where breaking is permissible, the proofs from the Quran and Sunnah, and the important clarification that only qada (make-up) is required, not fidyah. We also examine the condition that the fear must be based on dominant thought (ghalabat al-zann), experience, or expert medical advice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
The passage lists four situations: fear for the child's intellect/death/illness for pregnant or breastfeeding women, and fear for the mother's own health for both. State each scenario clearly.
The ruling is the same whether the child is biological or fostered.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
In all four scenarios, it is permissible (mubah) to break the fast. Some texts indicate it becomes wajib if harm is certain, but the concession is the key point.
The mufta bihi is that they only make up later, no fidyah.
3
Locate the primary proof
The first proof is Quran 2:184, which mentions a ransom for those who are able (with difficulty). The Hanafi school, following Ibn Abbas, includes pregnant and breastfeeding women in this category.
The word 'yutiqunahu' is interpreted as 'find it extremely difficult'.
4
Examine the supporting Hadith
A hadith from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud states that Allah has lifted the fast from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman, analogous to the traveler's concession for prayer.
This Hadith directly supports the permissibility of breaking the fast.
5
Understand the companion's saying
Ibn Abbas explicitly says: 'The pregnant and breastfeeding woman break and make up, and do not feed (no fidyah).' This is the relied-upon evidence for not requiring fidyah.
This overrides the apparent implication of the verse for those who are able.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وجه (۱) دلیل یہ آیت بھی ہے وعلى الذين يطيقونه فدية طعام مسكين (آیت ۱۸۴ سورۃ البقرۃ ) آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو طاقت نہ رکھتے ہوں وہ فدیہ دیں گے۔ اس آیت میں شیخ فانی داخل ہیں۔ لیکن ابن عباس کے قول کے مطابق حاملہ اور مرضعہ بھی داخل ہیں۔ عن ابن عباس وعلى الذين يطيقونه فدية طعام مسكين قال كانت رخصة للشيخ الكبير والمرأة الكبيرة وهما يطيقان الصيام ان يفطرا ويطعما مكان كل يوم مسكينا والحبلى والمرضع اذا خافتا. (ابوداؤد شریف، باب من قال هی مثبتة الشیخ والحیلی، ص ۳۳۸ نمبر ۲۳۱۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ حاملہ اور مرضعہ بھی ہر دن کے روزے کے بدلے فدیہ دیں گی۔ تاہم اتنی بات معلوم ہوئی کہ وہ روزہ چھوڑ سکتی ہے۔ (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن انس بن مالك رجل من بني عبد الله بن كعب ... احدثك عن الصوم او الصيام ان الله وضع عن المسافر شطر الصلوة وعن الحامل او المرضع الصوم او الصيام قالهما النبي علي كليهما او احدهما. (ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء في الرخصة في الافطار لحمل والمرضع ص ۱۸۱، نمبر ۷۱۵ ابو داؤد شریف ، باب من قال هی منقبة الشيخ والحبلی ص ۱۳۳۸، نمبر ۲۳۱۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ حاملہ اور دودھ پلانے والی عورتوں پر روزہ نہیں ہے۔ (۳) اور بعد میں قضا کرے گی اور فدیہ نہیں دے گی۔ اس کی وجہ یہ قول صحابی ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال تفطر الحامل والمرضع في رمضان وتقضيان صياما ولا تطعمان. (مصنف ابن عبد الرزاق، باب الحامل والمرضع ، ج رابع ، ص ۱۶۸ نمبر۷۵۹۴ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ وہ فدیہ نہیں دیگی بلکہ قضا کرے گی۔
ترجمه : (ا) اور خوف وہ معتبر ہے جو غلبہ بظن، یا تجربہ، یا مسلمان ماہر طبیب کے بتلانے سے حاصل ہو۔ تشریح : اس بات کا غالب گمان ہو کہ روزہ رکھنے سے بیماری بڑھ جائے گی، یادیر تک بیماری رہ جائے گی ، اس گمان کے بارے میں [1] یا تو بار بار کا تجربہ ہو کہ بیماری بڑھ جائے گی [۲] یا ماہر مسلمان طبیب ہو وہ کہے کہ بیماری بڑھ جائے گی ، یا بیماری دیر تک رہے گی تو اس کی بات مانی جائے گی ، خالی خولی گمان کافی نہیں ہے۔
(1110) And for the pregnant and breastfeeding woman if she fears deficiency of intellect or death or illness upon herself or upon her child, whether biological or foster. Translation: (1110) And for the pregnant woman, and the breastfeeding woman, when she fears deficiency of intellect, death, or illness upon herself or upon the child—whether the child is biological or foster. Explanation: There are four scenarios: [1] Pregnant woman fears that if she fasts, the child will suffer mental deficiency, death, or illness—she may break. [2] Breastfeeding woman fears same for child—she may break. [3] Pregnant woman fears for herself—mental deficiency, death, or illness—she may break. [4] Breastfeeding woman fears for herself—same—she may break. The ruling is the same whether the child is her own or foster-nursed. Proof: (1) The verse 'And upon those who are able to fast, a ransom of feeding a poor person' (Quran 2:184). From this verse, it is understood that those who lack the ability pay fidyah. This includes the elderly, and according to Ibn Abbas, also the pregnant and breastfeeding women. The narration of Ibn Abbas: 'It was a concession for the old man and old woman who are able to fast, that they may break and feed a poor person for each day, and the pregnant and breastfeeding if they fear.' (Abu Dawud) This shows that they can break. (2) Hadith of Anas ibn Malik: 'Allah has lifted from the traveler half the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman the fast'—the Prophet said both or one of them. (Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud) This shows that fasting is not required for them. (3) They must make up later and not pay fidyah. Evidence is the companion's saying: Ibn Abbas said: 'The pregnant and breastfeeding woman break in Ramadan and make up the fast and do not feed.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah) This shows they do not pay fidyah but make up. Translation: (a) And the considered fear is that which is based on prevailing thought, or experience, or the statement of a Muslim expert physician. Explanation: The dominant thought that fasting will worsen illness or delay recovery: [1] either from repeated experience that illness worsens, [2] or from a Muslim expert physician who says it will worsen or delay—then his statement is accepted; baseless thought is not sufficient.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage covers the Hanafi ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women regarding fasting in Ramadan. The text categorizes four situations: fear of harm to the child (mental deficiency, death, illness) or to herself, in both pregnancy and breastfeeding. In all these, it is permissible to break the fast. The proofs include Quran 2:184, interpreted by Ibn Abbas to include these women, a hadith explicitly stating that Allah has lifted the fast from them, and the companion's saying that they only make up and do not pay fidyah. The final part clarifies the definition of valid fear: it must be based on dominant thought (ghalabat al-zann), repeated experience, or the advice of a trustworthy Muslim physician. Mere speculation is insufficient. The Hanafi position is that these women break the fast upon genuine fear and later make up the missed days without any fidyah. This differs from some other schools that may require fidyah in addition to making up.

Key Terms
حامل (hamil): A pregnant woman who fears harm to herself or her baby if she fasts.
مرضع (murdi'): A breastfeeding woman, whether nursing her own child or a foster child.
غلبة بظن (ghalabat bi al-zann): A prevailing thought or strong belief, not certainty, that harm will occur. This is valid basis for breaking fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of fidyah for a pregnant woman who breaks her fast due to fear for her child?
A) She must pay fidyah and make up the fast
B) She must only make up the fast, no fidyah
C) She must only pay fidyah, no need to make up
D) She is exempt from both making up and fidyah

Key Takeaway
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break the fast upon genuine fear of harm, and the Hanafi school requires only qada, not fidyah.
