Commentary
This passage addresses the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the precise weight of the sa' used for Sadaqat al-Fitr. The matn states that the sa' is eight Iraqi ratls. The author explains that while all agree the sa' comprises four mudds, the weight differs: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it as eight ratls, while Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold it as five and one-third ratls. The author then presents proofs: For the eight-ratl view, the hadith of Anas ibn Malik where the Prophet performed wudu with two ratls and ghusl with a sa' of eight ratls, and the practice of 'Umar's sa' being eight ratls. For the five-and-one-third view, Abu Yusuf cites the hadith of Abu Hurayra describing the Medinan sa' as small, and the report of Malik estimating the Prophet's sa' at five and one-third ratls. The commentary notes that the majority of imams act upon the smaller sa'. The practical difference is that the amount of Sadaqat al-Fitr given will vary depending on which measurement is followed. The Hanafi school, however, follows the larger sa' of eight ratls as the basis for the quantity.

Key Terms
رطل (ratl): A unit of weight; in the Hanafi school, one Iraqi ratl equals approximately 130 dirhams (about 408 grams).
صاع (sa'): A measure of volume equal to four mudds; its weight is debated as above.
مد (mudd): One quarter of a sa'; a volume measure used for ritual purification and zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the sa' is eight Iraqi ratls for Sadaqat al-Fitr, based on the explicit hadith of Anas, despite the differing view of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Fajr Prayer and Standing at Muzdalifah
Ruling on Fajr prayer and standing at Muzdalifah with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for the Fajr prayer at Muzdalifah (timing and obligation), identify the boundaries of the standing place (excluding Batn Muhassir), and describe the recommended supplication.

Core Idea
Fajr at Muzdalifah is prayed early (ghalas); the entire plain except Batn Muhassir is for standing and fervent supplication.

This passage from the matn outlines the actions to be performed at Muzdalifah on the morning of the 10th of Dhul Hijjah. It specifies the timing of Fajr prayer, the extent of the standing area, and the manner of supplication. Understanding these rulings ensures the pilgrim correctly fulfills the wajib and sunnah of this stage of Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of actions and their legal status.
The text prescribes: (1) Fajr prayer at ghalas, (2) standing afterwards, (3) supplication. Determine the ruling level for each.
Fajr prayer itself is fard, but timing is sunnah. Standing is wajib. Supplication is sunnah.
2
Locate the proof for the timing of Fajr and the obligation of standing.
The Hanafi school relies on hadith evidence (e.g., from Ibn Mas'ud) showing the Prophet prayed Fajr early at Muzdalifah and then stood.
The specific hadith is cited in the dalil_block below.
3
Understand the exception: Batn Muhassir is excluded from the standing area.
The text explicitly states 'illa batn muhassir' – the entire Muzdalifah is a standing place except this valley.
This exclusion is agreed upon by all schools.
4
Describe the recommended supplication.
The pilgrim should stand 'mujtahidan fi du'aa'ihi' – striving in dua, asking for fulfillment of needs.
Intense and earnest supplication is sunnah.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf on the status of standing (wajib vs sunnah).
Some scholars consider it sunnah. Identify the opposing proof and the Hanafi response.
See the al_mukhtalaf_fih block for details.

After prayer, everyone stands in supplication until sunrise. The entire plain of Muzdalifah is a valid place for standing, except the valley of Muhassir. The pilgrim should be fervent in dua, asking for the fulfillment of worldly and religious goals.

Key Terms

غلس (ghalas): The time of early dawn before it becomes bright.

Rami Before Zawal on the Fourth Day
Ruling on permissibility of rami before zawal on the fourth day of Tashriq with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rami before zawal on the fourth day of Tashriq and identify the preferred time.

Core Idea
Rami before zawal is valid but better after; before sunrise is makruh.

This lesson unpacks the time-based rulings for rami on the fourth day of Tashriq (13th Dhu al-Hijjah). The matn establishes that rami becomes obligatory at dawn, then distinguishes between permissible, preferred, and disliked timings. The Hanafi position is supported by a hadith showing the Prophet performed rami after zawal, indicating the sunnah time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its obligation trigger
The act is rami (pelting) on the fourth day. The obligation (luzum) is triggered if the pilgrim is in Mina at dawn (fajr) on that day.
This condition ensures rami is due; if absent, no obligation.
2
Map the time rulings
The text gives three time windows: ja'iz (permissible) before zawal, afdal (better) after zawal, and makruh (disliked) before sunrise. Note the different hukm levels.
'Ja'iz' here means valid (sahih), not merely permissible.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith of Aisha (ra) shows the Prophet performed rami 'when the sun had passed its zenith' (idha zalat al-shams). This indicates the preferred time is after zawal.
The dalil is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its dalala is zanni, so it does not obligate the preferred time; it merely recommends it.
4
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that rami on the fourth day must be after zawal. The Hanafi school allows before zawal, citing flexibility from other evidence.
This difference affects practical planning for pilgrims.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a pilgrim performs rami at 10 AM (before zawal), it is valid (sahih) but not optimal. If done before sunrise, it is makruh tanzihi.
The mufta bihi is: permissible before zawal, but better after.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان طلع الفجر وهو بمنى فى الرابع لزمه الرمي (۱۳۷۴) وجاز قبل الزوال والافضل بعده وكره قبل طلوع الشمس (۱۳۷۵) تشریح : ۱۳ ذی الحجہ کو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک زوال سے پہلے رمی کرنا جائز ہے مگر افضل یہ ہے کہ زوال کے بعد کرے۔ البتہ سورج نکلنے سے پہلے رمی کرنا مکروہ ہے۔ وجه : (۱) اس حدیث میں اس کا اشارہ ہے کہ زوال کے بعد رمی کرے۔ عن عائشة قالت افاض رسول الله له من آخـر يـومـه حـيـن صـلـى الظهر ثم رجع الى منى فمكث بها ليالى ايام التشريق يرمى الجمرة اذا زالت الشمس ( ابو داؤد شریف ، باب فی رمی الجمار ص ۲۸۷، نمبر ۱۹۷۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ زوال کے بعد رمی کرے۔
And if dawn breaks while he is in Mina on the fourth [day], rami becomes obligatory for him (1374). And it is permissible before zawal, and better after it, and makruh before sunrise (1375). Explanation: On the 13th of Dhu al-Hijjah, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is permissible to do rami before zawal, but better after zawal. However, doing rami before sunrise is makruh. Evidence: (1) This hadith indicates that rami should be after zawal. Narrated by Aisha: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, departed from [Mina] on his last day when he prayed Zuhr, then returned to Mina and stayed there during the nights of the Days of Tashriq, pelting the jamrah when the sun had passed its zenith. (Abu Dawud, Chapter on Rami al-Jimar, p. 287, no. 1973) This hadith states that rami is after zawal.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It discusses the timing of rami on the fourth day of Tashriq (13th Dhu al-Hijjah). The matn states that if dawn breaks while the pilgrim is still in Mina on the fourth day, rami becomes obligatory. Then it gives the ruling: it is permissible to perform rami before zawal (noon), but it is better after zawal, and it is makruh (disliked) before sunrise. The commentary by the author explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, rami before zawal is valid (ja'iz) but the preferred time is after zawal, following the sunnah. The evidence (wajh) is a hadith from Aisha (radiallahu anha) which shows that the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) performed rami after the sun passed its zenith. This is a proof for the recommended time. The ruling on makruh before sunrise is based on other evidence. Note: The hanafi_position_box reflects that rami before zawal is sahih (valid) but afdal (better) after zawal.

Key Terms
رمی (ramy): Pelting the jamarat with pebbles during Hajj.
زوال (zawal): The moment when the sun passes its zenith (noon).
جائز (ja'iz): Permissible; the act is valid but not necessarily the best.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) After zawal
B) Before sunrise
C) During the night
D) After sunset

Key Takeaway
Rami on the fourth day before zawal is valid but better after; before sunrise is makruh.

Walking vs Riding for Rami
Ruling with proof on walking vs riding for rami

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on walking vs riding for rami and identify the supporting hadith.

Core Idea
Walk for rami when du'a follows; ride when it doesn't.

Performing rami (stoning the jamarat) is a required Hajj ritual. The manner of performing it—whether on foot or mounted—varies by day. This lesson clarifies the preferred method and its basis in the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine which day of rami is being discussed and what the recommended manner is (walking vs riding).
The text distinguishes between Yawm al-Nahr (10th) and Tashriq days (11th-13th).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited from Aisha (r.a.) describing the Prophet's practice.
Sunan Abi Dawud, no. 1973.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith indicates walking is better: the Prophet stood for du'a after the first two jamarat, implying he was on foot.
Riding would make prolonged du'a difficult.
4
Note the absence of ikhtilaf
This is a straightforward ruling without significant intra- or inter-madhhab dispute.
Other schools agree on the desirability of walking when du'a follows.
5
Apply the tarjih
The preferred position (mufta bihi) is to walk for the first two jamarat on Tashriq days for the sake of du'a, and riding is permissible elsewhere.
The tarjih authority is the practice of the Prophet ﷺ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و کل رمی بعده رمى ترميه ماشيا لتدعو بعده والا راكبا لتذهب عقبه بلا دعاء (۱۳۷۶) تشریح : دسویں تاریخ کو صرف جمرہ عقبہ کی رمی کرنی ہے اور اس کے بعد ٹھہرنا بھی نہیں ہے اس لئے سوار ہو کر رمی کرے تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے، اور دوسرے دن اور تیسرے دن اور چوتھے دن تینوں جمروں کی رمی کرنی ہے اس لئے اس میں بہتر یہ ہے کہ چل کر رمی کرتے جائے کیونکہ پہلی رمی اور دوسری رمی کے بعد ٹھہر کر دعا کرنی ہے اور دیر تک گڑ گڑا کر دعا کرنا چل کر آسان ہوگا کیونکہ سواری تو بھاگنے کی کوشش کرے گی اس لئے اس میں چل کر رمی کرنا افضل ہے۔ آج کل اتنی بھیڑ ہوتی ہے کہ سوار پر رمی کرنا مشکل ہے۔ وجه : (ا) عن عائشة قالت افاض رسول الله الله من آخر يومه حين صلى الظهر ثم رجع الى منى فمكث بها ليالي ايام التشريق يرمى الجمرة اذا زالت الشمس كل جمرة بسبع حصيات يكبر مع كل حصاة ويقف عند الاولى والثانية فيطيل القيام ويتضرع ويرمى الثالثة ولا يقف عندها (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی رمی الجمار، ص ۴۲۰، نمبر ۱۹۷۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ گیارہویں اور بارہویں کو پہلی اور دوسری رمی چل کر کرے اور دعا کرے اور تیسری رمی کے بعد کھڑا نہ ہو۔
And every rami after which there is another rami, you perform it walking to make du'a after it; otherwise riding to go quickly without du'a (1376). Explanation: On the 10th day, only the 'Aqabah jamrah is pelted and there is no staying after it, so if one does it riding there is no harm. On the second, third, and fourth days, all three jamarat are pelted, so it is better to do it walking, because after the first and second rami one stands and makes du'a for a long time, and walking makes that easier; riding tends to make one want to move on. Moreover, nowadays there is so much crowd that rami from a mount is difficult. Evidence: (1) Narrated by Aisha: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, departed from [Mina] on his last day when he prayed Zuhr, then returned to Mina and stayed there during the nights of the Days of Tashriq, pelting each jamrah with seven pebbles, saying takbir with each pebble, and he would stand at the first and second [jamrah] making long supplication, but at the third he did not stand. (Abu Dawud, Chapter on Rami al-Jimar, p. 420, no. 1973) This hadith shows that on the 11th and 12th, one walks for the first and second rami and makes du'a, and does not stand after the third.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof on the manner of performing rami. The text distinguishes between rami on the 10th (Yawm al-Nahr) and rami on the following days (Tashriq). On the 10th, only the Jamrah al-'Aqabah is pelted, and no standing for du'a is prescribed. Therefore, one may ride a mount if needed. On the 11th and 12th (and 13th for those staying), all three jamarat are pelted, and after the first and second, it is sunnah to stand for extended du'a. Hence it is better to walk, as riding would make it hard to stand and supplicate. The evidence is a hadith from Aisha describing the Prophet's (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) practice: he stood and made long supplication after the first two jamarat but not after the third. The Hanafi position is that walking is afdal (better) when there is a subsequent rami with du'a; otherwise riding is permissible.

Key Terms
ماشيا (mashiyan): Walking on foot.
راكبا (rakiban): Riding an animal or vehicle.
دعا (du'a): Supplication after rami at the first two jamarat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 11th, 12th, and 13th Dhu al-Hijjah
C) Only on the 13th
D) All days except the 10th

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: walk when du'a is prescribed after rami; ride when it is not.

Ruling on Staying Outside Mina During Tashriq Nights
Ruling with proof on staying overnight outside Mina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on staying overnight outside Mina during the nights of rami and explain the evidence used.

Core Idea
Staying outside Mina on Tashriq nights is makruh; the Prophet stayed in Mina and Umar prohibited leaving.

Many pilgrims assume that staying in Mina during the Days of Tashriq is merely recommended. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling: it is makruh (disliked) to spend the night outside Mina. We will examine the textual evidence from the Prophet's practice and Umar's directive, and understand the legal nuance behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The ruling is on 'al-mabit bi-ghayr Mina' (staying overnight outside Mina). The text explicitly states 'kariha' (it is makruh). Understand that makruh here means makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful) according to the author.
Some Hanafi scholars consider staying in Mina as wajib, but this text leans toward makruh.
2
Locate the First Dalil (Hadith)
The hadith from Aisha (radiallahu anha) shows that the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) returned to Mina after departing and stayed there during the nights of Tashriq, pelting the jamrah. This indicates the practice was regular.
Hadith from Abu Dawud, no. 1973.
3
Locate the Second Dalil (Companion Statement)
Umar ibn al-Khattab (radiallahu anhu) said: 'None of the pilgrims should stay overnight behind al-Aqabah during the nights of Mina.' This explicit prohibition from a Companion reinforces the ruling.
Narrated in Sunan al-Bayhaqi, no. 9690.
4
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The Prophet's continuous stay in Mina implies that it is the proper place; leaving it without excuse deviates from the sunnah. Umar's statement is a direct command (nahy) indicating prohibition. The Hanafi school uses such evidence to establish dislike (karahah) rather than outright prohibition.
The amr (command) from the Prophet's action and the nahy from Umar's statement combine.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars hold that mabit in Mina is wajib, and leaving without excuse requires a dam (sacrifice). The author here uses makruh, which may represent a weaker position. Know that the mufta bihi (operative ruling) varies; the most common view is that it is necessary (wajib) but the text treats it as makruh.
Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is consider it sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره المبيت بغیر منى ليالي الرمی (۱۳۷۷) ترجمه : (۱۳۷۶) اور رمی کی راتوں میں منی کے علاوہ ( دوسری جگہ ) رات گزارنا مکروہ ہے۔ وجه : (۱) اس حدیث میں ہے ۔ عن عائشة قالت افاض رسول الله له من آخر يومه حين صلى الظهر ثم رجع الى منى فمكث بها ليالى ايام التشريق يرمى الجمرة (ابوداؤ د شریف ، باب فی رمی الجمارص ۴۲۰ نمبر ۱۹۷۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور ایام تشریک میں منی میں ٹھہرے رہے۔ (۲) قال عمر بن الخطاب لا يبيتن أحد من الحاج ليالى منى من وراء العقبة - ( سنن بیہقی ، باب لا رخصتہ فی الستوتة بمکتہ لیالی منی ، ج خامس ،ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۹۶۹۰) اس قول صحابی میں ہے منی کی راتوں میں عقبہ کے پیچھے رات نہ گزارے۔
And staying overnight in other than Mina during the nights of rami is makruh (1377). Translation: (1376) And spending the night in a place other than Mina during the nights of rami is makruh. Evidence: (1) There is this hadith: Narrated by Aisha: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, departed from [Mina] on his last day when he prayed Zuhr, then returned to Mina and stayed there during the nights of the Days of Tashriq, pelting the jamrah. (Abu Dawud, Chapter on Rami al-Jimar, p. 420, no. 1973) This hadith shows that the Prophet stayed in Mina during the Days of Tashriq. (2) Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'None of the pilgrims should stay overnight behind al-'Aqabah during the nights of Mina.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter: No License to Stay Overnight in Makkah during Mina Nights, vol. 5, p. 249, no. 9690) This Companion statement shows not to spend the night behind 'Aqabah.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that it is makruh to spend the nights of rami outside Mina, i.e., not to stay in Mina itself during the nights of the 11th, 12th, and for some the 13th of Dhu al-Hijjah. The author gives two evidences: first, a hadith from Aisha (radiallahu anha) showing that the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) stayed in Mina during these nights; second, a statement of Umar ibn al-Khattab (radiallahu anhu) that pilgrims should not spend the night behind al-'Aqabah (i.e., outside the boundaries of Mina). The Hanafi position is that staying overnight (mabit) in Mina is wajib according to some, but here it is stated as makruh to stay elsewhere. The ruling is that leaving Mina without a valid excuse is makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful).

Key Terms
المبيت (al-mabit): Staying overnight in a place, specifically in Mina during the nights of Tashriq.
العقبة (al-'aqabah): The Jamrah al-'Aqabah or the area behind it, which marks the boundary of Mina.

Key Takeaway

Tawaf al-Wada' and Descending at Muhassab
Ruling on Tawaf al-Wada' with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of Tawaf al-Wada', explain its wajib status using the hadith of Ibn Abbas, and distinguish between those upon whom it is wajib (afaqi) and those not (Makkan residents).

Core Idea
Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib for pilgrims from outside Makkah; omitting it requires a dam.

This lesson covers the final rite of Hajj: the farewell circumambulation known as Tawaf al-Wada' or Tawaf al-Sadr. We will examine its legal status, the evidence from the Sunnah, and who must perform it. Understanding this ensures the pilgrim completes Hajj correctly and avoids a costly omission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is Tawaf al-Wada', a seven-circuit circumambulation of the Ka'bah performed after the days of Mina. The author states it is wajib (obligatory) for the afaqi (pilgrim from outside Makkah).
Note: The passage explicitly excludes the people of Makkah and residents there.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof: the hadith of Ibn Abbas (radiallahu anhuma) narrated in Bukhari and Muslim, where the Prophet ﷺ commanded that the last of the rites be at the House, with an exception for menstruating women.
The hadith is the primary evidence for obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith indicates wujub. The command 'an yakuna akhira 'ahdihim bi al-bayt' (the last of their rites should be at the House) uses an imperative, which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation (al-amr yufid al-wujub). The exception for menstruating women confirms the rule for others.
The imperative form is key to deriving wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that the Shafi'i school considers Tawaf al-Wada' as sunnah, not wajib. The Hanafi position relies on the apparent command, while the Shafi'i view considers the command as recommendation due to other contextual evidence.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf in the ummah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand that the Hanafi school adheres to the zahir (apparent meaning) of the command, supported by the practice of the Companions. The mufta bihi is that Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib, and omitting it without excuse requires a dam (blood sacrifice).
The tarjih authority is based on the strength of the dalil and Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اذا رحل الى مكة نزل المحصّب ساعة ثم يدخل مكة (۱۳۷۸ ) ويطوف بالبيت سبعة اشواط بلا رمل و لا سعى ان كان قد فعلهما قبل هذا طواف وداع و يسمى طواف صدر و هو واجب على الافاقى لا على اهل مكة و المقيمين بها. تشريح : يوضح الحكم. وجه : (۱) دلیل هذا الطواف حدیث ابن عباس قال امر الناس ان يكون آخر عهدهم بالبيت الا انه خفف عن الحائض ( بخاری شریف، باب طواف الوداع ، ص ۲۳۶، نمبر ۱۷۵۵ و مسلم شریف ، باب وجوب طواف الوداع وسقوطه عن الحائض ، ص ۴۲۷، نمبر ۳۲۱۹/۱۳۲۷) هذا الحدیث يدل على ان طواف الوداع واجب على الافاقي.
Then when he travels to Makkah, he descends at al-Muhassab for a while, then enters Makkah (1378). And he circumambulates the House seven circuits without raml and without sa'y if he had already done both before. This is Tawaf al-Wada', also called Tawaf al-Sadr. It is wajib upon the afaqi (one from outside Makkah), not upon the people of Makkah and those residing there. Explanation: [It clarifies the ruling.] Evidence: (1) The proof for this tawaf is the hadith of Ibn Abbas: 'The people were commanded that the last of their rites should be at the House, except that it was lightened for the menstruating woman.' (Bukhari, Chapter on Tawaf al-Wada', p. 236, no. 1755; Muslim, Chapter on Obligation of Tawaf al-Wada' and its Waiver for the Menstruating Woman, p. 427, no. 3219/1327) This hadith indicates that Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib upon the afaqi.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the final rites of Hajj after the days of Mina. The pilgrim, upon leaving Mina, should first stop at Muhassab (an area between Mina and Makkah) briefly, following the practice of the Prophet. Then he enters Makkah to perform Tawaf al-Wada' (farewell tawaf). This tawaf consists of seven circuits around the Ka'bah without raml (quick walking) and without sa'y (running between Safa and Marwah) if he already performed them earlier. The tawaf is called Tawaf al-Sadr or Tawaf al-Wada'. It is obligatory (wajib) for those who came from outside Makkah (afaqi), but not for the residents of Makkah. The evidence is the hadith of Ibn Abbas (radiallahu anhuma) where the Prophet commanded that the last rite should be the tawaf, except for menstruating women who are excused. This demonstrates its obligation. The Hanafi position is clear: Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib for afaqi; omitting it without excuse requires blood (dam).

Key Terms
طواف وداع (tawaf al-wada'): The farewell circumambulation of the Ka'bah performed after completing Hajj rituals before leaving Makkah.
المحصب (al-muhassab): A place between Mina and Makkah where pilgrims halt briefly before entering Makkah for tawaf al-wada'.
أفاقي (afaqi): A pilgrim who comes from outside the boundaries of the Haram, i.e., not a resident of Makkah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tawaf al-Wada' is a wajib rite for non-Makkan pilgrims; omitting it without excuse requires a dam.

Ruling on Drinking Zamzam Water with Intention
States ruling for drinking Zamzam with dua and intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on drinking Zamzam water, explain the proof from the hadith, and apply the principle of intention to this act.

Core Idea
Zamzam water is effective according to the drinker's intention — a sunnah of the Prophet ﷺ.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah addresses the recommended practice of drinking Zamzam water. It combines the Prophet's statement on intention with the specific dua of Ibn Abbas. Understanding this teaches us how adab (etiquette) transforms a simple act into worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is drinking Zamzam water. According to the Hanafi school, it is sunnah mu'akkadah to drink with intention and recite the dua.
The ruling comes from the hadith and companion practice.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith 'ماء زمزم لما شرب له' (Zamzam water is for what it is drunk for) is the textual evidence.
Recorded in Ibn Majah and Musannaf Abdurrazzaq.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لما شرب له' links the water's benefit to the drinker's intention, implying istihbab (recommendation) of intending a specific purpose.
The dalala is zanni, but accepted for fada'il.
4
Examine the companion practice
Abdullah ibn Abbas would recite a specific dua: 'O Allah, I ask You for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease.'
This shows the sunnah of making dua while drinking.
5
Apply the ruling
When drinking Zamzam, one should intend a specific benefit (e.g., cure, knowledge) and preferably recite Ibn Abbas's dua or a similar supplication.
Also recommended to pour some on oneself or wipe face.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The house, and he pours on his body if possible, otherwise wipes his face and head with it and intends whatever he wishes by drinking it. (1381) And Abdullah ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, when he drank, would say: O Allah, I ask You for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: Zamzam water is for whatever intention it is drunk. This hadith mentions that water is also taken home. Commentary
This passage is a ruling about drinking Zamzam water. It begins with the ruling that when one drinks Zamzam, he should pour it over his body if possible; if not, he should wipe his face and head with it, and he should intend whatever he wishes by drinking it. The author then cites the practice of Abdullah ibn Abbas, who would recite a specific dua: 'O Allah, I ask You for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease.' The Prophet's hadith is quoted: 'Zamzam water is for whatever intention it is drunk' — meaning its benefit is realized according to the drinker's intention. The text includes additional narrations about taking Zamzam water home and pouring it on clothes. The evidence section provides the exact chain for Ibn Abbas's dua and the hadith of Jabir confirming the principle. The ruling is mustahab (recommended) in the Hanafi school, and the dua is a sunnah practice.

Key Terms
زمزم (Zamzam): The sacred well in Mecca, from which pilgrims drink.
الملتزم (al-multazam): The area between the Black Stone and the Kaaba door where one clings and prays.

Key Takeaway
Drinking Zamzam water with intention and dua is a recommended sunnah; its benefit aligns with the drinker's sincere supplication.

Ruling on Visiting the Multazam
Describes recommended actions after drinking Zamzam at the multazam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended sequence of actions at the multazam after drinking Zamzam and explain the hadith evidence for this practice.

Core Idea
The recommended practice at the multazam is based on the Prophet's action, not an obligation.

After drinking Zamzam, the Hanafi school recommends a series of actions at the Kaaba door and the multazam. These actions are considered sunnah muakkadah, rooted in a hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar (or similar) describing the Prophet's own practice. This lesson walks through the sequence and its evidence, clarifying that it is recommended, not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Examine the passage: what is the act being described? The text states 'It is recommended after drinking it that he come to the door of the Kaaba and kiss the threshold, then come to the multazam...' Identify the hukm level: mustahab (recommended).
The term 'يُستحب' (yustahab) clearly indicates recommendation, not obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited in the source. The passage quotes a hadith from Amr ibn Shu'ayb narrating that Abdullah ibn Umar placed his chest, face, forearms, and palms on the multazam and stated he saw the Prophet do the same.
This is the primary proof for the recommended action.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling. The Prophet's action (fi'l) is not obligatory by default in Hanafi usul; it indicates recommendation (istihbab) unless there is evidence of obligation.
The Prophet's practice is a source of sunnah; his consistent action without rebuke implies recommendation.
4
Note any ikhtilaf
Check if other schools differ. The Hanafi position is accepted by most, but some may have variations regarding the exact manner or timing. The Hanafi school holds this as mufta bihi.
The main difference is whether to kiss the threshold or not; Hanafis consider it sunnah.
5
State the mufta bihi
Summarize the relied-upon position: after drinking Zamzam, go to the Kaaba door, kiss the threshold, then go to the multazam between the Black Stone and door, place chest and face, and cling to the curtain for a while.
This sequence is recommended for those about to leave Mecca.

(1382) It is recommended after drinking it that he come to the door of the Kaaba and kiss the threshold, then come to the multazam, which is between the Black Stone and the door, and place his chest and face upon it and cling to the curtains of the Kaaba for a while. Translation: (1382) And after drinking Zamzam, it is recommended that he go to the door of the Kaaba and kiss the threshold, then go to the multazam, which is between the Black Stone and the door, and place his chest and face on it and hold the curtain of the Kaaba for a little while. Explanation: The area from the Black Stone to the Kaaba door is called multazam; multazam means to cling, because people cling there. It is recommended that when he is about to return home, after drinking Zamzam, he come to the multazam, place his chest and face on it, and for a short while lie down with the hanging curtain of the Kaaba and weep. Evidence: The hadith for this is from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father who said: I performed tawaf with Abdullah; when we came to the back of the Kaaba, I said: Will you not seek refuge? He said: We seek refuge with Allah from the Fire. Then he went until he touched the Stone and stood between the corner and the door, and placed his chest, face, forearms, and palms like this, and stretched them out. Then he said: Thus I saw the Messenger of Allah do it. (Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah) This hadith mentions that the Prophet placed his chest and face on the multazam and spread his hands.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the recommended actions after drinking Zamzam water. The main text states that it is mustahab to go to the Kaaba door and kiss the threshold (al-utba), then proceed to the multazam—the area between the Black Stone and the door—and place one's chest and face against it, clinging to the Kaaba's curtain for a while. The Urdu commentary clarifies that 'multazam' literally means to cling, and it is the space from the Black Stone to the door. It further recommends doing this when one is about to leave Mecca, and to weep while lying against the curtain. The evidence is a hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar (or similar) describing how he placed his chest, face, and arms on the multazam, following the Prophet's example. The Hanafi school considers these actions as sunnah (recommended) for those performing Hajj or Umrah, especially at the time of farewell.

Key Terms
الملتزم (al-multazam): The area between the Black Stone and the Kaaba door where one clings in supplication.
العتبة (al-utbah): The threshold of the Kaaba door, which is recommended to kiss.

Supplication at the Multazam
Teaches the recommended supplication at the multazam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite the recommended supplication at the multazam and state the Hanafi ruling that it is a sunnah muakkadah.

Core Idea
Cling to the Multazam and pray with humility—it's a sunnah, not a fard.

The Multazam, the area between the Black Stone and the Kaaba door, is a place of profound spiritual connection. This lesson presents the recommended supplication for a pilgrim standing there, as taught by the Hanafi school. You will learn the specific dua, understand why it is a sunnah (recommended) and not an obligation, and see the proofs from the Prophet's practice and the early community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Recognise that the passage describes making supplication at the multazam and determines its hukm level. In Hanafi fiqh, this is a sunnah muakkadah (emphasised recommendation).
Sunnah muakkadah is an act the Prophet consistently performed but is not obligatory.
2
Locate the Dalil
The text cites two evidences: a hadith in Nasa'i about the Prophet facing the qiblah and praying, and a narration from Imam al-Shafi'i recommending a specific dua at farewell. These are the proofs for the recommendation.
Although Shafi'i narration is given, the Hanafi school also accepts this as a sunnah based on general practice.
3
Analyse the Wajh al-Dalalah
The hadith shows that the Prophet engaged in supplication at sacred sites, and the Shafi'i report provides the exact wording. For Hanafis, the continuity of practice (amal) strengthens the recommendation.
The ruling is derived from the Prophet's action (fi'l) which indicates sunnah, not obligation.
4
Memorise the Prescribed Du'a
Learn the supplication: 'O Allah, this is Your House...' and the additional personal requests. The text allows any good supplication, but the specific wording is preferred.
The du'a asks for acceptance, return, and Allah's pleasure.
5
Apply the Ruling in Practice
When you or a pilgrim are at the multazam (after tawaf), you should stand, face the qiblah, and raise your hands in humble dua. Recite the prescribed words or other sincere requests.
The act is valid without this specific du'a, but the full reward is attained by following the sunnah.

Al-Multazam: A Sacred Place for Answered Prayer
Lists multazam among fifteen places for dua.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-multazam as one of the fifteen places in Mecca where supplication is answered, according to the Hanafi school, and state the chain of transmission for this knowledge.

Core Idea
The multazam is one of fifteen sacred sites where du'a is accepted.

This passage introduces al-multazam as a specific location in Mecca where Allah answers supplications. It also references a longer list of fifteen such places transmitted by a respected Hanafi scholar from a renowned early authority. Understanding these places helps pilgrims maximize their devotions during Hajj or Umrah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term
Find the word 'al-multazam' in the text and note its definition: the area between the Black Stone and the door of the Kaaba.
Also note that it is a place of accepted prayer.
2
Recognise the Source Chain
Identify the two scholars mentioned: Kamal ibn al-Humam (later Hanafi jurist) and Hasan al-Basri (early Tabi'i). The knowledge of these places was transmitted through this chain.
This shows the importance of isnad even for spatial/virtue knowledge.
3
Understand the Significance
Reflect on why this matters in practice: making du'a at the multazam is a Sunnah during tawaf, and knowing it as a place of acceptance encourages devotion.
The Hanafi school considers it recommended to pray there.
4
Note the Incomplete Text
Observe that the passage cuts off mid-sentence. The full list of fifteen places is not provided here, but the source indicates such a list exists in Hasan al-Basri's treatise.
This reminds us that some texts reach us incomplete; we rely on other sources for the full list.

Ruling on Missing Rami (Stoning) During Hajj
Explains the compensation for missing rami with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the compensation (dam or sadaqah) for missing partial or complete rami on a single day of Hajj, and explain the principle of tadakhul when multiple days are missed.

Core Idea
Missing a full day's rami requires a dam; missing fewer pebbles requires sadaqah per pebble; three missed days merge into one dam.

In the Hajj rituals, rami (stoning the jamarat) is a wajib act. Missing it—whether partially or completely—requires compensation. This lesson details the graduated compensation: a charitable gift (sadaqah) for each missed pebble, but a blood sacrifice (dam) if an entire day's stoning is omitted. When all three days of tashriq are missed, the compensations merge (tadakhul) into a single dam. We will also examine the proofs from hadith and athar that establish these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
Rami (stoning) is the act of throwing pebbles at the three jamarat on the days of Tashriq. In the Hanafi school, it is classified as wajib (obligatory, not fard). Missing a wajib act in Hajj requires compensation (dam or sadaqah).
The hukm level determines whether compensation is needed and at what level.
2
Distinguish between full-day and partial omission
The passage states: if a pilgrim leaves all 7 pebbles of Jamrat al-Aqabah on the 10th, or all 21 pebbles of the three jamarat on the 11th, a dam is due. If fewer than the full day's quota is missed, each missed pebble incurs one sadaqah.
The threshold for dam is 'ramī yawm' — stoning of one full day (21 pebbles for 11th/12th, 7 for 10th).
3
Apply the rule of tadakhul
If all three days (10th, 11th, 12th) are missed, logically three dams would be required. However, due to tadakhul (intervention/merging), only one dam is owed because all omissions are of the same type (missing rami) and occur within the same overall duty.
Tadakhul lightens the burden; otherwise it would be excessive.
4
Examine the evidence
The Hanafi school relies on: (a) the athar of ‘Aṭā’ ibn Abī Rabāḥ that one dam suffices for missing one or all jamarat, (b) the comparison of rami to prayer in the athar of Abān ibn ‘Uthmān, and (c) the practical sunnah of the Prophet ﷺ performing rami. These indicate the wajib status and the compensations.
The proofs are from aḥādīth and āthār, hence zannī al-thubūt, which supports the hukm being wajib not fard.
5
Note practical application
If the value of the sadaqahs for missed pebbles reaches or exceeds the value of a dam, the amount is reduced to avoid turning into a dam inadvertently. This shows careful juridical precision.
The sadaqah per pebble is typically half a ṣā‘ of wheat or its value.

ثمرة النجاح جلد ٢ ۴۲۱ باب الجنايات يبلغ دما فينقص ما شاء (۱۴۳۸) او حلق رأس غيره او قص اظفاره ہے کہ ایک دن کی تمام جمرات چھوڑ دئے تو ایک دم لازم ہوگا ، اور اس سے کم چھوڑا تو ہر کنکری کے بدلے ایک ایک صدقہ لازم ہوگا۔ اب دسویں ذی الحجہ کو صرف جمرہ عقبہ کی کنکری مارنی ہے، پس اگر ایک کنکری بھی نہیں ماری تو دم لازم ہوگا ، اور ایک کنکری ماری اور باقی چھ نہیں ماری تو ۶ رصدقے لازم ہوں گے ۔ اگر اس صدقے کی قیمت ایک دم کو پہنچ جائے تو اس سے کچھ کم کر دیا جائے گا، تا کہ دم لازم نہ ہو جائے ۔ گیارہویں ذی الحجہ کو تینوں جمرات کی اکیس کنکریاں مارنی ہیں ، پس اگر سب چھوڑ دی تو دم لازم ہوگا ، اور اگر ایک ماری اور نہیں چھوڑ دی تو ۲۰ صدقے لازم ہوں گے ، اور اس کی قیمت ایک دم کو پہنچ جائے تو اس سے کم کر دیا جائے گا ۔۔ اور اگر تینوں دنوں کی کنکریاں چھوڑ دے تو تین دم لازم ہونا چاہئے لیکن تداخل ہو کر ایک ہی دم لازم ہوگا۔ وجه : (۱) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ تمام رمی چھوڑ دے تب بھی ایک ہی دم کافی ہے۔ عن عطاء بن ابی رباح انه قال مـن نـسـى جمرة واحدة او الجمار كلها حتى يذهب ايام التشريق فدم واحد يجزيه (سن للبیھقی ، باب من ترک شيئا من الرمی حتی يذهب ایام منی ص ۲۴۸، نمبر ۹۶۸۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک رمی چھوڑ دے تو دم لازم ہوگا ۔ یہ بھی معلوم ہوا کہ تمام رمی چھوٹ جائے تو تداخل ہو جائیں گے اور ایک ہی دم لازم ہوگا ۔ (۳) رمی جمار واجب ہے اس کے لئے یہ اثر ہے۔ عن الزهرى عن ابان ابن عثمان قال والله ان الصلوة لتقضى فكيف لا تقضى رمي الجمار (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ۴۰۵ فی الرجل ینسی ان برمی الجمار یقضیه او بحرق دما ، ج ثالث ص ۳۷۹ نمبر ۱۵۳۰۲) اس اثر سے رمی جمار کی اہمیت معلوم ہوتی ہے (۴) اس حدیث میں بھی ہے ۔ اخبر نا سلیمان ابن عمر بن الاحوص عن امه قالت رأيت رسول الله الله يرمى الجمرة من بطن الوادى و هو راكب يكبر مع كل حصاة - ابوداؤد شریف ، باب فی رمی الجمارص ۲۸۷ نمبر ۱۹۶۶ / ابن ماجہ شریف ، باب قدر حصی الرمی ، ص ۲۳۹، نمبر ۳۰۲۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور نے رمی کی جس سے واجب ہونے کا پتہ چلتا ہے (۵) اس حدیث میں بھی ہے۔ قال دخـلـنـا عـلـى جابر بن عبد الله ... ثم سلك الطريق الوسطى التي تخرج على الجمرة الكبرى حتى اتى الجمرة التي عند الشجرة فرماها بسبع حصيات يكبر مع كل حصاة منها - (مسلم شریف ، باب حجتہ النبی ص ۳۹۹ نمبر ۲۹۵۰/۱۲۱۸ / ابوداؤد شریف، باب صفۃ حجتہ النبی ص ۲۷۱ نمبر ۱۹۰۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ رمی کرے۔ لغت : كذالكل حصاة فيمالم يبلغ رمی یوم: ہر کنکری کے بدلے ایک ایک صدقہ لازم ہوگا ، جب تک کہ ایک دن کی رمی تک نہ پہنچ جائے ، پس اگر ایک دن کی رمی چھوڑ دے، مثلا اکیس کنکریاں چھوڑ دے تو دم لازم ہوگا۔
Thamarat al-Najah Volume 2, page 421, Chapter of Offenses. 'It reaches a blood sacrifice, then reduces as he wishes. Or he shaves another's head or cuts his nails.' It is that if he leaves all the jamarat of one day, then one dam is obligatory. And if he leaves less than that, then for each pebble one charity is obligatory. On the 10th of Dhul-Hijjah, only the pebbles of Jamrat al-Aqabah are to be thrown. So if he does not throw even one pebble, a dam is obligatory. And if he throws one pebble and does not throw the remaining six, then 6 charities are obligatory. If the value of that charity reaches one dam, then it is reduced somewhat so that a dam does not become obligatory. On the 11th of Dhul-Hijjah, 21 pebbles are to be thrown at the three jamarat. So if he leaves all, a dam is obligatory. And if he throws one and does not leave the rest, then 20 charities are obligatory. If its value reaches one dam, then it is reduced. And if he leaves the pebbles of all three days, then three dams should be obligatory, but tadakhul (intervention) occurs and only one dam becomes obligatory. Wajh (Proof): (1) In this statement of a Tabi'i: 'If he forgets one jamrah or all the jamarat until the days of Tashriq pass, then one dam suffices him.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on leaving some of the rami until the days of Mina pass, p. 248, no. 9688) From this athar, it is known that if he leaves one rami, a dam is obligatory. It is also known that if all rami is missed, then tadakhul occurs and one dam is obligatory. (3) Rami of the jamarat is wajib, and for this there is this athar: From al-Zuhri from Aban ibn Uthman who said: 'By Allah, prayer is made up, so why is rami of the jamarat not made up?' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 15302) This athar indicates the importance of rami of the jamarat. (4) In this hadith: 'Sulayman ibn Umar ibn al-Ahwas narrated to us from his mother who said: I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) throwing pebbles at the jamrah from the bottom of the valley while riding, saying takbir with each pebble.' (Abu Dawud, no. 1966; Ibn Majah, no. 3028) This hadith indicates that rami is wajib. (5) In this hadith: 'We entered upon Jabir ibn Abdullah... then he took the middle road that leads to the great jamrah until he came to the jamrah by the tree, and he threw seven pebbles, saying takbir with each pebble.' (Muslim, no. 1218; Abu Dawud, no. 1905) This hadith shows that rami is performed. Lugah (Lexicon): 'Ka dhalika kullu hasatin fima lam yablugh rami yawm': For each pebble, one charity is obligatory, until it reaches the rami of one day. So if he leaves the rami of one day, e.g., 21 pebbles, then a dam is obligatory.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the consequences of missing rami (stoning the jamarat) during Hajj, with proofs from hadith and athar. The text begins by stating the general principle: if one omits all the stoning of a single day, a dam (blood sacrifice) is obligatory; if less than a full day's worth is omitted, each missed pebble requires a sadaqah. The author then specifies the days: on the 10th of Dhul-Hijjah only Jamrat al-Aqabah is stoned (7 pebbles), so missing all 7 requires a dam, missing some requires sadaqah per pebble, but if the total sadaqah reaches the value of a dam, it is reduced to avoid a dam. On the 11th, three jamarat are stoned (21 pebbles total), and the same rule applies. If all three days are missed, logically three dams would be due, but tadakhul (intervention) applies, meaning only one dam is required. The proofs include: (1) an athar from Ata ibn Abi Rabah that missing one or all jamarat after the days of Tashriq requires one dam; (2) an athar from Aban ibn Uthman comparing rami to prayer in terms of making up; (3) a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) performing rami while riding and saying takbir; (4) a description of the Prophet's rami action. The lexicographical note clarifies that 'ka dhalikakullu hasatin' means each pebble incurs a sadaqah until it reaches the amount of a full day's rami (21 pebbles), at which point a dam becomes obligatory. The Hanafi position is that rami is wajib (not fard), and missing it requires compensation.

Key Terms
دم (dam): A blood sacrifice (usually a sheep or goat) required as compensation for missing a wajib act in Hajj.
صدقہ (sadaqah): A charitable donation, here of food or its value, given as compensation for minor omissions in Hajj.
تداخل (tadakhul): The merging of multiple required compensations into one, due to the common cause or time frame.
رمی (rami): The act of throwing pebbles at the jamarat during Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pilgrim misses all stoning on the 11th of Dhul-Hijjah, what compensation is required?
A) One dam
B) 20 sadaqahs
C) One sadaqah per missed pebble
D) No compensation needed

Key Takeaway
Missing a full day's rami requires a dam; partial omission requires sadaqah per pebble; if all three days are completely missed, tadakhul reduces the total to one dam.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Sending Salawat during Ziyarah: Proofs that the Prophet Hears
Introduction to ziyarah etiquette with proofs of the Prophet's hearing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sending salawat when visiting the Prophet's grave and explain the proofs that the Prophet hears salawat.

Core Idea
The Prophet ﷺ hears salawat at his grave; send abundant salawat when visiting.

When visiting the Prophet's grave, many neglect the proper etiquette. This lesson explains the strong recommendation to send abundant salawat and the evidence that the Prophet ﷺ hears it or it is conveyed to him. We will examine the Quranic and hadith proofs that affirm the Prophet's life in the grave and his ability to hear salutations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: it is sunnah mu'akkadah to send abundant salawat when intending to visit the Prophet's grave.
The text explicitly says 'yanbaghi an yukthira min al-salat 'alayhi' – it is befitting to send many salawat.
2
Understand the basis
Recognize that the ruling is based on the belief that the Prophet hears salawat if said near his grave, and that salawat from afar is conveyed to him.
The author cites hadith from Bayhaqi to establish this.
3
Examine the Quranic proof (qiyas awla)
Analyze the Quranic verses (3:169-171, 2:154) which state that martyrs are alive and provided for. By qiyas awla (greater analogy), prophets are even more alive in their graves.
The argument: if martyrs are alive, prophets (who are higher) must be alive.
4
Examine the hadith proofs
Review the hadiths: (1) The Prophet's soul is returned to respond to salam (Abu Dawud). (2) The earth does not consume the bodies of prophets (Abu Dawud). (3) The dead hear the footsteps of departing people (Bukhari). These support the Prophet's ability to hear.
These hadiths are used to affirm that the Prophet can hear salawat.
5
Grasp the ikhtilaf and tarjih
Note that some later scholars deny the Prophet's hearing in the grave. The Hanafi school gives tarjih to the specific proofs over general principles.
The thamarah al-khilaf: for Hanafis, sending salawat at the grave is sunnah; for deniers, it is not special.

باركت على آل ابراهیم فی العالمین انک حمید مجید ، و السلام صلى الله عليه وسلم
Chapter on visiting the Prophet ﷺ with all pleasures and devotions, except that he is veiled from the sight of those deficient in noble stations. (1466) And when we saw that most people are heedless of fulfilling the right of his visitation and what is sunnah for visitors of general and particular matters, we wished to mention after the rites and their performance some etiquette to complete the benefit of the book. (1467) So we say: It is befitting for one who intends to visit the Prophet ﷺ to send abundant salawat upon him, for he hears it and it is conveyed to him. Mention of verses and hadith. (1468) And its virtues are more famous than to mention. (Then Urdu commentary explains each proof that the Prophet is alive, hears salams, bodies of prophets are preserved from earth, etc. The section ends with the hadith on how to send salawat).
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a new chapter on visiting the Prophet ﷺ. The author first explains his motivation: seeing people neglect the proper etiquette of ziyarah, he includes this section after Hajj to complete the book's benefit. The ruling is that one should send abundant salawat upon the Prophet ﷺ when intending visitation, because the Prophet hears the salawat if said near his grave, and from a distance it is conveyed to him. The author provides several proofs: (1) Quranic verses (3:169-171, 2:154) showing that martyrs are alive and provided for, and by greater reason prophets are alive in their graves. (2) Hadith: 'No one sends salam upon me except that Allah returns my soul to me so that I respond to the salam' (Abu Dawud). (3) Hadith: 'Allah has forbidden the earth to consume the bodies of prophets' (Abu Dawud). (4) Quran 39:30 indicates the Prophet will die in a worldly sense, but this does not negate life in the grave. (5) The author cites a hadith from Bayhaqi: 'Whoever sends salawat upon me at my grave, I hear him; whoever sends salawat from afar, it is conveyed to me.' He then supports the possibility of hearing by citing hadith where the dead hear the footsteps of departing people, and where the Prophet addressed the dead of Badr. Finally, he notes the virtue of salawat is well-known, citing the Quranic command (33:56) and the hadith teaching the manner of sending salawat. The overall tone is that of a scholar teaching fiqh al-ziyarah and defending the belief in the Prophet's life in the grave based on textual and rational proofs.

Key Terms
زيارة (ziyarah): Visiting the grave of the Prophet ﷺ with the intention of honoring him and seeking blessings.
درود (salawat): Blessings and salutations sent upon the Prophet ﷺ, often phrased as 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'.
شهداء (shuhadā'): Martyrs who are killed in the path of Allah; the Quran states they are alive but we perceive it not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Recite the Quran loudly so that the Prophet hears.
B) Send abundant salawat upon the Prophet because he hears or it is conveyed to him.
C) Remain silent and avoid any supplication.
D) Give charity on behalf of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
When visiting the Prophet's grave, send abundant salawat, for he hears it or it is conveyed, proven by Quran and hadith.

Recommended Acts for Entering Madinah and the Prophet's Mosque
Describes recommended acts for visiting Madinah and Masjid al-Nabawi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended etiquettes for entering Madinah and Masjid al-Nabawi, explain the Hanafi proof for each act, and identify the legal level (sunnah/mustahabb).

Core Idea
Entering Madinah and the Prophet's Mosque with humility and specific supplications is sunnah, rooted in hadith and analogy.

Visiting the Prophet's city and mosque is a profound act of devotion. The Hanafi school outlines specific etiquettes drawn from the practice of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) and the early Muslims. This lesson unpacks the recommended acts for a visitor, emphasising their merit and the evidence behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify each recommended act
Read the passage and list the specific actions described: entering Madinah on foot, reciting a specific supplication, entering Masjid al-Nabawi, praying tahiyyat al-masjid near the minbar, positioning with the minbar pillar aligned to the right shoulder, praying an additional two rak'ahs as sajdat al-shukr, and proceeding to the grave with reverence.
Each act has a distinct legal character—tahiyyat al-masjid is sunnah mu'akkadah, while the additional prayer is mustahabb.
2
Determine the legal level
For each act, note the legal classification. The text uses phrases like 'if possible' and 'recommended', indicating these are sunnah (voluntary) acts, not obligatory. The Hanafi position box confirms the hukm level as sunnah.
The absence of a command (amr) in imperative form suggests non-obligation.
3
Locate the proof for each act
Find the evidence cited: 1) Analogy (qiyas) for the virtue of entering Madinah: bathing for entering Makkah is extended to Madinah because it is also a sanctuary (haram). 2) Hadith for the area between the Prophet's house and pulpit being a garden of Paradise (Muslim). 3) Hadith for prayer in Masjid al-Nabawi being better than a thousand elsewhere (Bukhari/Muslim). 4) Hadith for the minbar being over the Prophet's cistern (Muslim).
The proofs are ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut), which support sunnah rulings.
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
For each hadith, identify the specific wording that indicates virtue or recommendation. E.g., the phrase 'what is between my house and my minbar is a garden of Paradise' encourages prayer there, but does not command it. The word 'better than a thousand prayers' highlights reward, not obligation.
The Hanafi principle: a statement of virtue (fadilah) does not create obligation unless accompanied by a command.
5
Synthesize the practice
Combine all acts into a sequence: enter Madinah on foot → recite supplication → enter mosque → pray tahiyyat al-masjid at the minbar position → pray two rak'ahs of thanks → proceed to the grave. Note that tahiyyat al-masjid is sunnah for any mosque, and the extra prayer is a voluntary expression of gratitude.
The sajdat al-shukr is performed as two rak'ahs (nafl) rather than a single prostration; this is a specific Hanafi practice for this context.

Chapter on Visiting the Prophet (peace be upon him): (1471) Then he enters Madinah walking if possible without necessity, after placing his belongings and being assured of his servants and goods, humbly with tranquility and dignity, observing the majesty of the place. (1472) Saying: 'In the name of Allah and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah, O Allah, cause me to enter a true entrance and exit a true exit, and grant me from Yourself a supporting authority. O Allah, bless our master Muhammad and the family of Muhammad, etc., and forgive my sins and open for me the doors of Your mercy and bounty.' (1473) Then he enters the noble mosque and prays its greeting. The hadith about bathing for entering Makkah is analogized to Madinah because it is also a sanctuary. (Urdu translation and explanation follow.) Then he enters Masjid Nabawi and prays two rak'ahs of tahiyyat al-masjid near the pulpit. Evidence: Hadith from Abd Allah ibn Zayd al-Mazini: 'What is between my house and my pulpit is a garden from the gardens of Paradise.' And from Abu Hurayrah: 'A prayer in my mosque is better than a thousand prayers elsewhere except the Sacred Mosque.' (1474) He stands such that the pillar of the noble pulpit is aligned with his right shoulder, for that is the standing place of the Prophet. And what is between his pulpit and grave is a garden of Paradise, and his pulpit is over his cistern. (1475) Then he performs a prostration of thanks by praying two rak'ahs other than tahiyyat al-masjid, thanking Allah for enabling him to reach this place, then he makes whatever supplication he wishes. (1476) Then he rises to proceed toward the noble grave, standing at a distance of four cubits from the chamber with utmost reverence.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on visiting the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) in Madinah. It outlines the recommended etiquettes (adab) for a visitor. The rulings are based on hadith and qiyas (analogy). The passage first instructs entering Madinah on foot if possible, demonstrating humility. The supplication upon entry includes Quranic verses. Then it describes entering Masjid al-Nabawi and praying tahiyyat al-masjid near the pulpit, citing the hadith that the area between the Prophet's house and pulpit is a garden of Paradise. It also mentions the virtue of prayer in this mosque being better than a thousand prayers elsewhere (except the Sacred Mosque). The visitor should position himself so the pulpit pillar aligns with his right shoulder, as that was the Prophet's standing place. After tahiyyat, he prays an additional two rak'ahs as a prostration of thanks (sajdat al-shukr) for being blessed to visit. Finally, he proceeds toward the grave with utmost respect. The proofs are from Sahih Muslim and Sahih Bukhari. The Hanafi position regards these actions as sunnah (recommended), not obligatory, as indicated by the phrase 'if possible' and the absence of a command.

Key Terms
تحية المسجد (tahiyyat al-masjid): Two rak'ahs of nafl prayer offered upon entering a mosque, as a greeting to the mosque.
سجدة شكر (sajdat al-shukr): A prostration of thanks performed when receiving a blessing or being saved from calamity. Here it is prayed as two rak'ahs of nafl.

Performing Two Rak'ahs at the Minbar of the Prophet's Mosque
Ruling and proof for prayer at the minbar in the Prophet's Mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for standing at the minbar in the Prophet's Mosque and describe the prescribed sequence of prayer and sujud shukr.

Core Idea
Two rak'ahs at the minbar's pillar spot are a sunnah of visiting Madinah, followed by a separate sujud shukr.

Visiting the Prophet's Mosque in Madinah is a deeply meritorious act. This lesson details the specific sunnah prayer to be performed near the minbar, including the exact standing position and the subsequent prostration of gratitude. Understanding this practice helps preserve the transmitted tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is praying two rak'ahs at the minbar. The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized recommended). Also followed by sujud shukr (mustahabb).
The sujud shukr is gratitude for reaching the mosque.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith stating that the area between the Prophet's grave and minbar is a garden of Paradise, and his minbar is upon his cistern.
The specific spot (pillar opposite right shoulder) is from the Prophet's practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit statement designates the area as a garden, indicating high merit and encouraging prayer there.
The hadith encourages seeking blessings in that area.
4
Distinguish between actions
Note that the two rak'ahs are separate from tahiyyat al-masjid; they are performed as a gratitude for reaching the mosque.
The text explicitly says 'ghayr tahiyyat al-masjid'.
5
Evaluate the authority
The ruling is from later fatawa works but based on authentic hadith. The Hanafi school emphasizes the specific spot.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin.

منبره رکعتین (۱۴۷۴) ويقف بحيث يكون عمود المنبر الشريف بحذاء منكبه الايمن فهو موقف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وما بين قبره ومنبره روضة من رياض الجنّة كما أخبر به صلى الله عليه وسلم وقال منبری علی حوضی (۱۴۷۵) فتسجد شكرا لله تعالى باداء ركعتين غير تحية المسجد شكرا لما وفقك الله تعالى ومنَّ علیک بالوصول اليه ثم تدعو بما شئت (۱۴۷۶) ثم تنهض
Chapter on visiting the Prophet (peace be upon him). Better than a thousand prayers elsewhere? Two rak'ahs by his minbar (1474). And he stands such that the pillar of the noble minbar is opposite his right shoulder, for that is the standing place of the Prophet (SAW). And what is between his grave and his minbar is a garden from the gardens of Paradise, as he informed, and he said: 'My minbar is upon my cistern' (1475). Then he performs a prostration of gratitude to Allah by performing two rak'ahs besides the greeting of the mosque, gratitude for Allah granting him success and favoring him with reaching it. Then he supplicates as he wishes (1476). Then he rises.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the etiquette of visiting the Prophet's Mosque in Madinah. The author begins by stating the virtue of praying two rak'ahs at the minbar, which is better than a thousand prayers elsewhere, as indicated by the hadith. The specific ruling is that one should stand such that the pillar of the minbar aligns with the right shoulder, because that is the exact spot where the Prophet (SAW) used to stand when leading prayer. The area between the grave and the minbar is confirmed as a garden of Paradise via explicit hadith. After these two rak'ahs, one performs an additional two rak'ahs as a prostration of gratitude (sujud shukr) for being granted the opportunity to visit, distinct from the tahiyyat al-masjid. This is followed by any supplication. The passage then transitions to rising to proceed to the grave.

Key Terms
منبر (minbar): The pulpit in the Prophet's Mosque from which the Prophet delivered sermons.
سجدة شكر (sajdat shukr): A prostration of gratitude performed upon receiving a blessing, such as visiting the Prophet's mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Etiquette of Visiting the Prophet's Grave (Ziyarah)
Prescriptive sequence for ziyarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended sequence and supplications for visiting the graves of the Prophet, Abu Bakr, and Umar according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Visiting the Prophet's grave is a sunnah; follow the prescribed sequence of greeting, moving, and supplicating at each grave.

This passage outlines the proper etiquette for visiting the Prophet's grave in Medina, a highly recommended act (sunnah muakkadah) in the Hanafi school. It details the specific actions and supplications to be performed at the graves of the Prophet, Abu Bakr, and Umar, emphasizing the importance of following the sunnah sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall act and its ruling
Recognize that visiting the Prophet's grave (ziyarah) is a sunnah muakkadah (highly recommended) according to the Hanafi school. The passage describes the manner of performing this visitation.
This is not an obligatory act but a meritorious one.
2
Outline the sequence at the Prophet's grave
The passage instructs: seek intercession (tashaffu'), then send blessings upon the Prophet while facing his grave with your back to the qiblah, then make any supplication you wish.
The direction (back to qiblah) is specific to the Prophet's grave.
3
Outline the supplication for Abu Bakr
Move about an arm's length to the side until opposite Abu Bakr's head, then recite the salutation praising his virtues as caliph and companion.
The salutation is lengthy and honors his role.
4
Outline the supplication for Umar
Move similarly to be opposite Umar's head, then recite the salutation praising his conquests and support of Islam.
Similar structure but specific to Umar's achievements.
5
Note the final supplication and departure
After the graves, supplicate for yourself and others, then depart.
The visitation concludes with general dua.

Section on visiting the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. Seek his intercession with your Lord, so he may intercede for him and the Muslims. Then send blessings upon him and make whatever supplication you wish at his noble face, turning your back to the qiblah. Then move about an arm's length until you are opposite the head of as-Siddiq Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, and say: Peace be upon you, O Caliph of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. Peace be upon you, O companion of the Messenger of Allah, his intimate in the cave, his companion in journeys, his confidant in secrets. May Allah reward you with the best reward that He has rewarded any imam from his community for his prophet. Indeed, you succeeded him in the best manner, followed his path and his way in the best manner, fought the apostates and innovators, established Islam and strengthened its pillars. You were the best imam, maintained family ties, and always stood for truth, supporting the religion and its people until certainty came to you. Ask Allah, the Glorious, for us to have constant love for you, to be resurrected with your party, and to accept our visitation. Peace be upon you and the mercy and blessings of Allah. Then move similarly until you are opposite the head of the Commander of the Faithful, Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, and say: Peace be upon you, O Commander of the Faithful. Peace be upon you, O upholder of Islam. Peace be upon you, O breaker of idols. May Allah reward you with the best reward. Indeed, you supported Islam and the Muslims, conquered most lands after the Master of the Messengers, cared for orphans, maintained family ties, and Islam was strengthened by you. You were for the Muslims a pleasing imam, a guided guide. You united their ranks and helped their poor. Then supplicate for yourself and for whomever you wish, and depart. (The actions of visitation are taken from books of rituals.)
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a detailed instruction for the etiquette of visiting the Prophet's grave in Medina, followed by visiting the graves of Abu Bakr and Umar. It begins by stating that one should seek the Prophet's intercession, then send blessings upon him while facing his grave and having one's back to the qiblah. Then, moving about an arm's length to the side, one stands opposite the head of Abu Bakr and recites a lengthy salutation praising his virtues. Then, moving similarly, one stands opposite Umar's head and recites a similar salutation. The passage ends with a general instruction to supplicate for oneself and others. The text is prescriptive, describing the sunnah manner of visitation as derived from the books of rituals. The supplications are specific and emphasize the unique roles of Abu Bakr as caliph and companion, and Umar as the conqueror and upholder of Islam. The commentary in the original book provides Urdu translations and additional hadith evidence, but the core ruling is the sequence of actions and set prayers.

Key Terms
زيارة (ziyarah): Visiting the grave of the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, with the intention of seeking blessings and sending salutations.
تشفع (tashaffu'): Seeking intercession from the Prophet by asking him to supplicate to Allah on one's behalf.
صدّيق (siddiq): Title of Abu Bakr, meaning 'the truthful one', referring to his confirmation of the Prophet's Isra' and Mi'raj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Visiting the Prophet's grave is a sunnah; follow the prescribed sequence: greet the Prophet, then move to greet Abu Bakr and Umar, then make personal supplication.

Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Supplications at the Graves of Abu Bakr and Umar
Instructions and specific du'as for ziyarah of the caliphs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite the prescribed du'as for visiting the graves of Abu Bakr and Umar in the correct order and explain the Hanafi ruling on their recitation as sunnah.

Core Idea
Visiting the graves of Abu Bakr and Umar and reciting specific du'as is a recommended sunnah, rooted in love for the Prophet's companions.

This passage provides the exact du'as to recite when visiting the graves of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, Abu Bakr and Umar, after honoring the Prophet. The Hanafi school regards these du'as as part of the established sunnah of the Salaf, enhancing the spiritual benefit of ziyarah. Understanding the sequence and legal status ensures proper adab and avoids misconceptions about their necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of positions
The visitor first faces the grave of Abu Bakr, recites the du'a for him, then moves to align with Umar's head and recites his du'a, then steps back half a cubit to address both together.
The order is fixed and should be followed step by step.
2
Analyze the structure of each du'a
Each du'a begins with a salutation (salam), followed by praise of the caliph's virtues, then a supplication for acceptance and blessing.
Notice the use of honorific titles like 'Amir al-Mu'minin' and 'Muzhir al-Islam'.
3
Recognize key terms and concepts
Terms like 'ḍajīʿ' (sleeping companion) and 'tawassul' (seeking intercession) are central. Understand that tawassul is permissible and recommended in Hanafi thought.
These terms reflect the deep respect for the companions.
4
Determine the hukm (legal ruling)
Reciting these du'as is sunnah (recommended), not wajib. The visit itself is valid without them, but they enhance the reward.
This distinguishes them from obligatory acts and prevents confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في زيارة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم

(۱۴۹۰) ثم تتحوّل مثل ذلك حتى تُحاذى رأس امير المؤمنين عُمر بن الخطاب رضی الله عنه فتقول السلام علیک یا امیر المؤمنین السلام علیک یا مظهر الاسلام السلام علیک یا مکسر الاصنام جزاك الله عنا افضل الجزاء لقد نصرت الاسلام والمسلمين وفتحت معظم البلاد بعد سيّد المرسلين وكفلت الايتام ووصلت الارحام وقوى بك الاسلام و كـنـت للمسلمين اماما مرضيًا وهاديا مهديًا جمعت شملهم وأعنت فقيرهم وجبرت كسيرهم السلام علیک ورحمة الله وبركاته

(۱۴۹۲) ثم يدعو لنفسه ولوالديه و لمن اوصاه بالدعاء ولجميع المسلمين
Chapter on Visiting the Prophet (peace be upon him). [Du'a for Abu Bakr:] 'May Allah reward you, O Imam, on behalf of his nation. You have truly succeeded him in the best manner, followed his path and way in the best way, fought the people of apostasy and innovation, established Islam, strengthened its pillars, you were the best Imam, maintained family ties, and you never ceased standing for truth, supporting the religion and its people until certainty came to you. Ask Allah, glorified, for us enduring love for you, resurrection with your group, and acceptance of our visit. Peace be upon you and the mercy of Allah and His blessings.' (1490) Then move similarly until you are opposite the head of the Commander of the Faithful Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) and say: 'Peace be upon you, O Commander of the Faithful; peace be upon you, O one who manifested Islam; peace be upon you, O breaker of idols. May Allah reward you with the best reward from us. Indeed, you supported Islam and the Muslims, conquered most lands after the Master of Messengers, sponsored orphans, maintained family ties, Islam was strengthened by you, and you were for the Muslims a pleasing Imam, a guiding and guided leader, you united their ranks, helped their poor, and mended their broken. Peace be upon you and the mercy of Allah and His blessings.' (1491) Then you retreat about half a cubit and say: 'Peace be upon you both, O sleeping companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), his friends, ministers, advisors, helpers in establishing the religion, and those who after him managed the affairs of Muslims. May Allah reward you both with the best reward. We have come to you seeking your intercession to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) so that he may intercede for us and ask Allah, our Lord, to accept our efforts, give us life on his path, cause us to die on it, and resurrect us in his group.' (1492) Then he supplicates for himself, for his parents, for those who asked him to supplicate, and for all Muslims.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a practical instruction manual for the ziyarah (visitation) of the graves of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, Abu Bakr and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them), which is performed after visiting the grave of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The text is presented in the style of a matn (core text) to be memorized. It begins with a du'a specific to Abu Bakr, praising his role in preserving Islam after the Prophet's death, his battles against apostasy, his establishment of the religion, and his just leadership. The visitor asks Allah to accept the visit and grant love for Abu Bakr. Then (1490), the visitor moves sideways to align with the head of Umar's grave and recites a du'a that acknowledges Umar's title as 'Commander of the Faithful,' his manifestation of Islam through conquests, his care for orphans, his strength to Islam, and his role as a guiding leader. Next (1491), the visitor steps back half a cubit (about 24 cm) so that both graves are before him, and addresses both Abu Bakr and Umar together, referring to them as the 'sleeping companions' of the Prophet. The du'a seeks their intercession to the Prophet, asking for his intercession and for Allah to grant a good end on the Prophet's path. Finally (1492), the visitor makes a general supplication for himself, his parents, those who requested his prayer, and all Muslims. The sequence emphasizes order and precise positioning. In the Hanafi school, these du'as are considered recommended (sunnah) and form part of the etiquettes of visiting Madinah. They are not obligatory but highly meritorious, reflecting deep love and respect for the Prophet's companions.

Key Terms
ضجیع (ḍajīʿ): Literally 'one who lies down beside'; here it refers to Abu Bakr and Umar as those buried next to the Prophet (peace be upon him) in his chamber.
توسل (tawassul): Seeking means of approach; here it means asking the righteous (the companions) to intercede with the Prophet (peace be upon him) on behalf of the visitor.
زيارة (ziyārah): The act of visiting graves with the intention of greeting the deceased, making supplication, and deriving spiritual benefit. Here it specifically means visiting the Prophet's mosque and the graves of the companions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Leave the cemetery immediately

Key Takeaway
Reciting these supplications at the graves of the caliphs is a non-obligatory but highly recommended act in the Hanafi school, reflecting deep respect for the Prophet's companions.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Standing at the Prophet’s Grave and Supplicating
Describes the recommended supplication at the Prophet's grave with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended du'a at the Prophet's grave, identify its Quranic sources, and explain the Hanafi ruling on this practice.

Core Idea
The du'a at the Prophet's grave is a sunnah mu'akkadah, rooted in Quranic verses that encourage seeking intercession.

This lesson unpacks a vital aspect of ziyarah: the specific supplication taught by the Hanafi school to be recited at the Prophet's ﷺ grave. We'll see how the madhhab derives this practice directly from the Quran, recognizing it as a stressed sunnah rather than an obligation, and understand why the visitor is encouraged to add personal prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, recognize that the act described is standing at the head of the Prophet ﷺ and reciting a specific du'a. The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah, not wajib.
2
Locate the Dalil
Identify the Quranic verses cited: 4:64, 59:10, 2:201, 37:180-182. These form the textual basis for the du'a.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Analyze how each verse supports the practice. For example, verse 4:64 indicates that coming to the Prophet and seeking forgiveness through his intercession is a means of divine mercy.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Some other schools discourage specific du'as at the grave, considering them innovations. The Hanafi school allows them based on the Quranic evidence and early practice.
5
State the Mufta Bihi
The operative ruling is that the practice is recommended (mandub) and meritorious, with the allowance to add any personal supplications.
